Opening statement from the authors 
In December 2018 the 24th Conference of the Parties (COP24) was held in Katowice, Poland. 
While the participating countries managed to settle on how to measure, report and verify their 
efforts towards the agreements made in Paris three years prior, it was not discussed if and 
how the countries will strive for even more ambitious targets. The current target levels for the 
world is set to 2 degrees of warming from pre-industrial levels, although the 
Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) stress the importance of limiting the 
global temperature increase to 1.5˚C (Fiona Harvey, 2018). At the time of the agreement 
made in Paris, relative little scientific knowledge existed about the difference between 1.5˚C 
and 2˚C global warming, but since then a report has been released on the consequences of a 
temperature increase greater than 1.5˚C. The report highlights that political will is key for the 
implementation of solutions that both improve well-being and make it possible to limit global 
warming to 1.5˚C (IPCC Secratariat, 2018). 
  
The motivation for this Master’s thesis was found in the realisation that quantifying 
sustainability with relative measures is not suitable if we are to develop towards a world with 
a temperature increase of no more than 1.5˚C since pre-industrial level. While conventional 
Life Cycle Assessments are useful for comparative matters and for assessing eco-efficiency, 
they cannot indicate whether or not we stay within the safe operating space – i.e. whether our 
actions are enough. According to the Danish Transportation, Construction and Housing 
Authority buildings in Europe contribute to 20-30% of the most crucial damages made to the 
environment and 30% of waste generated in Denmark is connected to the built environment 
(Trafik og Byggestyrelsen, 2016).  
 
In Denmark, sustainability in buildings can be demonstrated by the achievement of a DGNB 
(Deutche Gesellschaft für Nachhaltiges Bauen) certification, in which the completion of a 
LCA accounts for 13% of the certification score (Denmark, 2016). Although all 
improvements matter, it is crucial to consider if we are improving enough, thus inspiring the 
need for measures of absolute sustainability. While the fact that human activities pose a risk 
to the state of the Earth System is not new (Meadows et al., 1972; Ryberg, 2018), the idea of 
coupling LCA with absolute measures is both new and, at this point, still an experimental 
approach. 10 years has passed since Rockström published the Planetary Boundaries-
framework and since then different attempts at incorporating this into LCA as both 
==PAGE:4==
 
 
  
iv 
characterisation, normalisation and weighting factors have been done. However, actually 
applying the developed methods to assess human activities relative to the Planetary 
Boundaries have only been done in few case studies and therefore calls for more work. This 
thesis seeks to continue the existing work of others in the field of absolute environmental 
sustainability assessments and to contribute to the understanding of how LCA can be coupled 
with absolute measures.  
 
This thesis is a study on assessing the level of absolute sustainability of six single family 
stand-alone dwellings using the two methods carrying capacity-based normalisation factors 
(CCnorm) and Planetary Boundary-based Life Cycle Impact Assessment (PB-LCIA). The first 
part of the study is an Article concerning the main methodological choices and conclusions of 
the study. The second part of the study (Supporting Information I to V) serves as supporting 
information to the Article. Hence, the Article is possible to read as an independent piece, 
whereas the Supporting Information should be read in connection with the Article.  
 
Following Supporting Information III, we have included a closing statement containing our 
final thought as a follow up to this opening statement.

1 
Assessment of absolute sustainability in the built environment 
Camilla Andersen1, Pernille Ohms1  
 
Supervisors: 
Freja Nygaard Rasmussen2, Harpa Birgisdottir2, Morten Birkved3, Michael Hauschild4 & 
Morten Ryberg4 
 
1Technical University of Denmark, Department of Civil Engineering, Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark 
2The Danish Building Research Institute, Copenhagen, Denmark 
3Southern University of Denmark, Institute of Chemical Engineering, Biotechnology and 
Environmental Technology, Odense, Denmark 
 4Technical University of Denmark, Department of Management Engineering, Kgs. Lyngby, 
Denmark 
 
ABSTRACT 
The purpose of this study is to investigate absolute sustainability in the built environment, by 
assessing whether or not current approaches to building design, and thereby the consumption 
of resources and subsequent emissions, can be considered within the carrying capacity of the 
environment.  
Two methods for absolute environmental sustainability assessment were applied; one where 
the normalisation step was done with carrying capacity based-normalisation factors and one 
where characterisation of elementary flows was done with characterisation factors based on 
the Planetary Boundaries. A life cycle assessment (LCA) was conducted for six houses to 
quantify their environmental footprints. Finally, allocation principles were applied to 
determine the share of the safe operating space that a single family stand-alone dwelling 
should be assigned. This allows for an assessment of environmental impact of each house in 
an absolute perspective.  
In this study it is found that the approaches tested in the Quota House and Upcycle House, 
namely reducing the energy consumption and recycling and reuse of materials, have the 
greatest potential to reach an absolute sustainable level and that the two applied methods 
overall provide the same conclusions. However, the conclusions drawn from the analysis are 
found to be dependent of the applied allocation principle used to assign a share of the safe 
operating space. From this study it can be concluded that improvements in the current 
approach to sustainable building design are still needed to reach absolute sustainability in the 
built environment.  
 
==PAGE:7==
 
2 
KEYWORDS  
Environmental performance of buildings, absolute sustainability assessment, carrying 
capacity, Planetary Boundaries 
 
1 INTRODUCTION 
Today, there is enough evidence to ascertain a clear connection between human activities and 
changes in the Earth’s climate due to increasing concentrations of CO2, CH4, and other 
greenhouse gases (Steffen et al., 2004). The emission of greenhouse gases linked to human 
activities have increased since before the industrialisation and can be explained by a growth 
in both economy and population (IPCC et al., 2014). While the world population have 
increased more than 10-fold to 7.6 billion over the past three centuries, the rate of 
consumption has increased even more dramatically (Steffen et al., 2004). In fact, human 
activities have escalated so rapidly since the mid-20th century that the ecological stability of 
the 11,700-year-long period known as the Holocene is now threatened (Steffen et al., 2015). 
Concerns regarding the shift from the Holocene-state have led to efforts to quantify local, 
regional, and global thresholds that should be respected for the Earth to be able to maintain 
the, to humans, necessary ecosystem services (Bjørn et al., 2014). 
 
The building sector has a strong influence on total natural resource consumption and on the 
emissions released and is thus a crucial stakeholder when seeking to decrease the total 
resource consumption. Life cycle assessment (LCA) methods have been used for 
environmental evaluation in product development processes in other industries for a long 
time (Bjørn et al., 2018), although application to the building sector has increased in the 
recent years (Brejnrod et al., 2017).The comprehensive and systematic approach of LCA has 
led to an increasing interest in incorporating LCA methods into building construction 
decision making, as well as for evaluating and optimising construction processes (Cabeza et 
al., 2014). LCA is a relative environmental assessment framework and most often used in 
comparative assessments, i.e. to claim environmental superiority of one product over another, 
or in hotspot analysis’ to identify the main environmental impacts of a product’s life cycle 
(Brejnrod et al., 2017; Ryberg, 2018). Thus, LCA is particularly suitable as a tool for 
decision support (Hauschild, Rosenbaum and Olsen, 2018), but cannot be used to indicate if a 
product is truly sustainable (Ryberg, 2018). Hence, to tell if the Earth System processes are 
able to cope with the burdens of human activities, coupling LCA with absolute measures is 
necessary.  
==PAGE:8==
 
3 
In this study, two absolute measures are considered, namely the carrying capacity and the 
Planetary Boundaries of the Earth System. The carrying capacity of Earth System processes 
is defined as “the maximum sustained environmental intervention a natural system can 
withstand without experiencing negative changes in structure or functioning that are difficult 
or impossible to revert” (Bjørn and Hauschild, 2015). The carrying capacity expresses the 
environmental capacity of local, regional or global Earth System processes (Brejnrod et al., 
2017) and defines the boundary between sustainability and unsustainability (Bjørn and 
Hauschild, 2015). However, due to a lack of scientific knowledge about the nature of 
biophysical thresholds, uncertainty regarding how the actual systems behave, inherent 
feedback mechanisms and uncertainty in the amount of time a critical control variable may be 
exceeded, the defined carrying capacity is placed in a zone of uncertainty (see Figure 1) 
(Rockström et al., 2009). Bjørn and Hauschild (2015) defined the carrying capacity as the 
medium or average value, corresponding to a medium level of precaution whenever an 
uncertainty range or confidence interval was given for an identified threshold. To ensure that 
the carrying capacity of the Earth System is not exceeded, target levels, namely the Planetary 
Boundaries, have been defined in academia and are now commonly referred to by 
governments and industries (Ryberg, Owsianiak and Hauschild, 2018). The Planetary 
Boundaries were first launched in 2009 (Rockström et al., 2009) and are defined as the lower 
limit of the zone of uncertainty, corresponding to a high level of precaution. Thus, the 
Planetary Boundaries framework defines the safe operating space (SOS) for humanity for the 
area of protection known as the Holocene state of the Earth when the natural background 
level are accounted for (see Figure 1) (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018). The full 
SOS (i.e. the Planetary Boundary or carrying capacity when the natural background level is 
subtracted) can be found in Supporting Information I, Section II.II and II.III.  
==PAGE:9==
 
4 
 
Figure 1 – The concept of Planetary Boundaries and carrying capacity framework for global 
Earth System processes. The green zone marks the safe operating space, the yellow zone the 
uncertainty and the red zone a high risk of critical change. The lines mark the Planetary 
Boundary (Rockström et al., 2009) located at the lower limit of the zone of uncertainty and 
the carrying capacity located as an average in the zone of uncertainty as defined by Bjørn 
and Hauschild (2015) 
 
This study seeks to investigate absolute sustainability of single family stand-alone dwellings 
and evaluate whether or not the current approaches to sustainable building design can be 
considered ‘truly sustainable’. To do so, two methodologies, carrying capacity-based 
normalisation factors (CCnorm) and Planetary Boundaries-based Life Cycle Impact 
Assessment (PB-LCIA), are applied to a case study of six single family stand-alone dwellings 
and a share of the global capacity of the environment is allocated to each dwelling. Finally, it 
is assessed whether or not our current approach to design of single family stand-alone 
dwellings is ambitious enough or should be reconsidered in order to be absolute sustainable 
according to the applied absolute environmental sustainability assessment methods. 
 
2 MATERIALS AND METHODS  
Absolute environmental sustainability assessments differ from traditional LCAs (which are, 
as described in Section 1, inherently a relative tool) by the incorporation of control variables 
and thresholds. To assess whether or not an activity can be regarded as being 
environmentally sustainable, its impact scores, i.e. the activity’s footprint, must be related to 
==PAGE:10==
 
5 
an absolute environmental sustainability threshold (Ryberg, 2018). Next, it is essential to 
define a relevant environmental boundary for the assessed activity and finally allocate a share 
of the boundary to the assessed activity. If the footprint of the activity is smaller than the 
allocated share of the relevant boundary, the activity can be regarded as absolute 
environmental sustainable (Ryberg, 2018). Further details on absolute environmental 
sustainability assessments can be found in Supporting Information I, Section II.I.  
 
2.1 The two applied methods  
Attempts to incorporate absolute sustainability in a LCA context has in the past been in the 
form of weighting factors. Hauschild, Wenzel and Alting (1998) presented carrying capacity-
based distance-to-target weighting factors and Tuomisto et al. (2012) attempted to adapt the 
Planetary Boundaries (Rockström et al., 2009) as weighting factors for eight impact 
categories. However, weighting has the disadvantage of allowing for tradeoffs between 
impact categories, hence it is possible to exceed the threshold for one impact category if 
others are sufficiently reduced (Bjørn and Hauschild, 2015).  
 
 
To avoid subjectivity, we applied two novel approaches for absolute sustainability 
assessment. Ryberg et al. (2018) have recently presented characterisation factors adapted to 
the Planetary Boundaries, thereby proposing a way of expressing impacts from a 
conventional LCA in the metrics of the Planetary Boundaries (Ryberg, Owsianiak, 
Richardson, et al., 2018). One of the main challenges of this adaptation is the fact that 
conventional LCIAs are integrated over a time period (e.g. the service life of a product) and 
thus the full life cycle impact cannot be measured in the current environment, rendering them 
unsuitable for determining if an environmental threshold is exceeded (Ryberg, Owsianiak, 
Richardson, et al., 2018). Hence, the time in which the impacts occur is important, as one 
pulse emission may lead to the ecosystem reaching its capacity at once, whereas the same 
emission occurring over long time may only occupy a small share of the capacity (Ryberg, 
2018). The capacity of each Earth System is inherently dynamic due to natural changes in the 
environment and to human interventions affecting the ecosystem temporarily or permanently 
(Bjørn and Hauschild, 2015). Hence, the carrying capacity of an ecosystem should ideally be 
assessed at any given point in time. Figure 2 shows that LCI modelling for the PB-LCIA 
framework can be compared to conventional LCI modelling, as the framework assumes that 
the elementary flows from all life cycle stages will occur in an “average” year, thereby 
continuously fulfilling the functional unit.  
==PAGE:11==
 
6 
 
 
Figure 2 - Difference between conventional LCA-study and PB-LCIA. The figure is adapted 
from Ryberg et al. (2018) and has been modified to fit the framework structure for building 
LCAs. 
 
Prior to the work by Ryberg et al. (2018), Bjørn and Hauschild (2015) proposed to use 
carrying capacity as an environmental sustainability reference in the normalisation step of 
LCAs. The work by Bjørn and Hauschild (2015) addresses the fact that “as common 
normalisation factors are solely based on activities within the technosphere they cannot be 
used to compare and aggregate the severity of different types of interventions in the 
ecosphere”. This motivation led to the development of European and global carrying 
capacity-based normalisation factors compatible with characterised indicator scores at 
midpoint-level for impact categories that link to the Area of Protection ”Natural 
Environment” (Bjørn and Hauschild, 2015). However, as the normalisation factors are not 
compliant with those of ILCD 2011 Midpoint+ (ILCD 2011), European Commission’s Joint 
Research Centre (Sala et al., 2016) translated the normalisation factors to be compliant with 
ILCD 2011 impact categories in order to facilitate the method to be used more widely. 
 
We applied the two methods to test the robustness of our conclusions. The goal was not to 
compare the results obtained from the two methods, as they are inherently not comparable (in 
some cases they do not consider the same impact categories and when they do consider the 
same impact categories, it is modelled differently in the two approaches), but to investigate 
whether the two methods support the same overall conclusion.  
 
 
==PAGE:12==
 
7 
2.2 Allocating the safe operating space 
To investigate whether or not an activity can be considered absolute sustainable, it is 
necessary to determine if it occupies a larger share of the safe operating space than what it 
should be assigned. The subjects of consideration in this paper are all single family stand-
alone dwellings and it was therefore necessary to determine the share of the safe operating 
space (SoSOS) available to a building of this type, as the Planetary Boundaries and carrying 
capacity both indicate the SOS available in total. Allocating a share of the global limit to a 
smaller entity can be based on different principles such as maximising the utility of the global 
population (i.e. utilitarian based), seeking equality between all people (i.e. egalitarian based), 
or distributing relative to current contribution to environmental impact (i.e. acquired rights 
based) (Ryberg, Owsianiak and Hauschild, 2018).  
 
For this study we wished to apply different allocation principles as a way of testing the 
robustness of our assessment, as Ryberg et al. (2018) found that the choice of allocation 
principle has great influence on how big a share of the SOS a system occupies. We 
determined six combinations of allocation principles by utilising both egalitarian, utilitarian 
and acquired rights-based approaches. All allocation principles can be seen in Table 1. It 
should be noted that the allocation principles applied in this study only represent a selection 
of ways the safe operating space can be shared. They do not cover all possible ways of 
allocating the safe operating space, and others could be just as reasonable as the ones applied 
in this study.

the share allocated to a single family stand-alone dwelling, are calculated by multiplying the 
fractions across each row. The calculated shares can be found in Supporting Information I, 
Section III.III. Further details on how the shares were calculated can be found in Supporting 
Information I, Section II.IV.

Where Popworld is the world population, FCEHH is the final consumption expenditure for a 
household, FCEperson is the final consumption expenditure for a person, FCEdwe is the final 
consumption expenditure for a dwelling, N is the number of persons in a household, Hhome is

CO2world is the CO2 emitted worldwide. All factors are considered on an annual basis. This 
study distinguishes between a household and dwelling. A person’s household includes all 
home-based activities and products, such as cooking, cleaning, relaxing, furniture etc., while 
dwelling refers to the building, and only the activities and products related to the building 
itself, i.e. paying rent, floorings, major appliances etc.  
 
To determine if the studied dwellings can be considered absolute sustainable we calculate the 
share that each dwelling occupies of the SoSOS, by applying the allocation share to the full 
SOS of either the Planetary Boundary or carrying capacity as shown in Equation 1 and 2:

PB-LCIA is a characterised impact score calculated with Planetary Boundary-based 
characterisation factors developed by Ryberg (2018), and SOSPB,i is the full SOS of the 
Planetary Boundaries when considering an impact category, i (see Supporting Information I, 
Section II.II). CIS is the characterised impact score calculated using ILCD 2011 divided by 
SOSCC,world,i which is the full SOS when considering the global annual carrying capacities for 
an impact category, i, also noted as the carrying capacity-based normalisation factors (see 
Supporting Information I, Section II.III) (Brejnrod et al., 2017). ASi is the allocation shares 
calculated according to the allocation principles presented in Table 1. SoSOSi represents the 
share of the safe operating space of an impact category i allocated to a single family stand-
alone dwelling of the total SOS. If the share is less than 1, a dwelling can be considered 
within its SoSOS and therefore absolute sustainable.  
 
2.3 Case study 
In this study we investigated five houses known as the Mini CO2 Houses (for more details on 
the project see Supporting Information I, Section II.V) and compared these to a sixth house 
==PAGE:15==
 
10 
(referred to as the Reference House (Ref House)), which reflects standard Danish building 
practice in 2015 and serves as a benchmarking point in this study. The Mini CO2 Houses 
were designed to each reduce one known source of CO2 emissions in buildings to an extreme 
degree, i.e. one design parameter is highlighted and optimised while all other parameters are 
designed to simply comply with (at that point of time) current building regulations, enabling 
a comparison between the different parameters and showing the impact of different actions 
towards CO2 reductions. 
The first, Upcycle House (UP House), focuses on how recycling by adding value can save 
resources and decrease CO2 emissions during construction of buildings. The Innovative 
Maintenance-free House (IMF House) and the Traditional Maintenance-free House (TMF 
House) excel in two areas; maintenance and lifespan. The two houses differ by the choice of 
materials. TMF House is built from traditional materials that are known to have a long 
lifespan, while IMF House is built from new materials that are expected to have a long 
lifespan. Both houses have a theoretical lifespan of 150 years and a building envelope that 
does not require any maintenance during the first 50 years of use. The concept of the 
Adaptable House (AD House) is flexibility and variability during its lifespan. The house is 
made up of components that are possible to disassemble, providing the opportunity to expand 
the house without destroying existing and remaining components. The final house, Quota 
House (Q House) focuses on smart technology, the behaviour of the residents and their 
consumption patterns (Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir, 2013; Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2013a, 
2013b, 2014a, 2014b; Realdania Byg and Leth & Gori, 2014). 
All modelled systems contain parameters that are likely to change in the future, such as 
energy consumption, disposal routes, and energy mix. We identified five key characteristics 
of the buildings which current trends point towards as likely development paths in the coming 
years (Backhaus et al., 2011). Therefore, we defined 5 scenarios each representing a version 
of the future where one characteristic is highlighted. We modelled the change in each 
characteristic as a “positive” environmental development, i.e. contributing to a (expectedly) 
lower environmental impact than the current conditions (Base Scenario), thereby representing 
a more sustainable version of the future. Finally, we defined a sixth scenario where all 
characteristics are changed and thus represents a best case scenario for our planet. It should 
be noted that the characteristics from Scenario 1 to 5 are mutually exclusive and can be 
combined without conflicting assumptions.  
==PAGE:16==
 
11

modifications. All improvements are based on the study by SPREAD Sustainable Lifestyles 
(Backhaus et al., 2011) and an extensive description of the scenarios can be found in 
Supporting Information I, Section II.VI.

2.4 Life Cycle Assessment  
The LCAs of the six houses described in Section 2.3 was carried out following ISO 
14040:2006, ISO 14044:2006 and DS/EN 15978:2011 (Dansk Standard, 2006, 2008, 2012). 
In the following subsections a description of aspects relevant to the modelling process will be 
given.  
 
2.4.2 Scope definition 
In typical LCAs, defining the functional unit (FU) includes defining for how long the 
function is provided (Hauschild, Rosenbaum and Olsen, 2018). In this case, the duration 
would be the lifetime of the building (either 120 or 150 years). However, to reflect the 
framework described in Section 2.1 the inventory must express elementary flows as 
continuous constant input per unit of time rather than an input integrated over time (Ryberg, 
Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018). The FU of the compared systems was defined as to 
==PAGE:17==
 
12 
annually house one family in a stand-alone dwelling in Denmark. It was assumed that the 
reference flow necessary to fulfil the FU is one Mini CO2 House. All modelled systems are 
located in Nyborg, Denmark. The life cycle inventory (LCI) data used to model the 
foreground system are representative for a Danish context, and parameters such as energy 
mix, energy consumption, and disposal routes were all modelled according to current 
conditions in Denmark. All systems were modelled using the software tool SimaPro 8.0 (PRé, 
2019) with the database ecoinvent 3.4 (Ecoinvent, 2019). Further details regarding scope 
definition can be found in Supporting Information I, Section II.VII.I.  
 
2.4.3 Life Cycle Inventory  
The data used to model the inventory were supplied by the Danish Building Research 
Institute and all inventories can be seen in Supporting Information IV. The provided data 
only cover building components, and all inventory data regarding Construction, Use, and End 
of Life were therefore based on either Environmental Product Declarations (EPDs), relevant 
literature, or assumptions. A full list of all assumptions regarding the construction of the 
inventories can be found in Supporting Information III.   
 
The uncertainty of the inventory was considered quantitatively by carrying out a Monte Carlo 
analysis. Uncertainties regarding assumptions were considered qualitatively. In addition, the 
sensitivity of the Base Scenario model was tested towards the changes made in Scenario 1-6. 
The relative change was calculated and used as a measure of sensitivity in the model. It was 
assumed that if the relative change is greater than 2% the model can be considered sensitive. 
Furthermore, a normalised sensitivity coefficient (Owsianiak et al., 2018) was calculated for 
a 10% change in district heating consumption, water consumption, energy for construction 
and the lifespan of the buildings (for further details, see Supporting Information I, Section 
II.VII.II). Since the normalised sensitivity coefficient represents the change in impact divided 
by the change in input and is thus a factor between 0 and 1, it was assumed that if the 
normalised sensitivity coefficient is greater than 0.5 for a single impact category, the 
parameter can be considered sensitive. Similarly, a parameter was seen as sensitive if the 
average of the sensitivity coefficients for all impact categories is greater than 0.3. Further 
details can be found in Supporting Information I, Section II.VII.II.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:18==
 
13 
2.4.4 Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA)  
Two different impact assessment methods were applied in this study. These are thoroughly 
described in Section 2.1. The impact coverage choice was based on a preliminary literature 
review carried out by the authors of this study (see Supporting Information II). The impact 
assessment of this study focuses on four impact categories from the LCIA-method ILCD 
2011, namely Climate change (kg CO2 eq), Marine eutrophication (kg N eq), Land use (kg C 
deficit), and Water resource depletion (m3 water eq) and five impact categories for the 
Planetary Boundary based LCIA-method developed by Ryberg et al. (2018), namely Climate 
change - energy imbalance (Wm-2), Climate change - CO2 concentration (ppm), Land system 
change - global (%), Biogeochemical flows - N (Tg N), and Freshwater use - global (km3). It 
was decided to include impact categories related to climate change, land use and water use as 
the preliminary literature review indicated that the latter two is relevant in studies concerning 
the built environment while the former is highly relevant in almost all studies, as climate 
change is a widely discussed topic in most political arenas. The impact categories related to 
nitrogen was included as they represent an important contributor to anthropogenic pressures 
that can have serious consequences for ecosystem functioning (Desmit et al., 2018). Further 
details regarding choice of impact coverage can be found in Supporting Information I, 
Section II.VII.III. 
 
3 RESULTS  
In order to make the results transparent and comparable to similar building studies, the 
characterised results obtained using ILCD 2011 are presented in Table 3.  
 
 
==PAGE:19==
 
14 
 
Table 3 - Characterised annual impact potential for each of the Mini CO2 houses for the 
Base Scenario using the LCIA method ILCD 2011. The lowest impact potential is marked in 
green, the highest in red.

From the results presented in Table 3, it is found that Q House has the lowest impact potential 
in the Base Scenario for 4 out of 4 of the selected impact categories, whereas AD House is 
found to have the highest impact potential for 3 out of 4 impact categories. It is found that the 
low environmental impact potential for Q House is due to a considerably lower energy and 
water consumption during the Use stage, whereas the high environmental impact potential for 
AD House is due to more materials being used when expanding the house several times 
during the service life. In general, it is found that the main contributor to the environmental 
impact potential is the Use stage in all impact categories except those related to nitrogen. For 
impact categories related to climate change and land use, the environmental impact emerges 
from the consumption of electricity and district heating in the use stage, whereas for the 
impact categories related to water use, the environmental impact is caused by the 
consumption of water during the Use stage. As a consequence of the consumption of water, a 
great amount of wastewater needs to be handled in the End of Life stage, which greatly affect 
the environmental impact in impact categories related to nitrogen (Marine eutrophication and 
Biogeochemical flows – N). For further details on the main contributors to the environmental 
impact, see Supporting Information I, Section III.II.

the two methods are presented in Figure 3 and 4. The Figures show the range of which each 
house exceed the allocated SoSOS when considering the six different allocation principles 
and the inherent uncertainty of the model.

impact categories, six different allocation principles and using the method CCnorm. The red 
line indicates the boundary, which means that if the impact score is above the line, hence 
above 1, the boundary is exceeded and vice versa. The error bars indicate the combined 
uncertainty of the model as well as the range in allocation principles, where the latter 
dominate the uncertainty. The results are shown on a logarithmic scale. For Water resource 
depletion the uncertainty range is only expressed from the mean to the upper limit of the

The results obtained using the method CCnorm show that the allocated share of carrying 
capacity is definitely exceeded for all Mini CO2 houses in 1 out of 4 impact categories and 
possibly exceeded in 3 out of 4 impact categories considered in our study (see Figure 3). As 
found, AD House has the greatest impact potential and exceeds the assigned share of the 
carrying capacity by averagely 700% across allocation principles for Climate change, 
whereas Q House exceeds the assigned share of carrying capacity by an average of 400% 
across allocation principles for Climate change. As for the impact category Marine 
eutrophication, Figure 3 shows that for most allocation principles the Mini CO2 Houses are 
likely to stay within the assigned share of carrying capacity. However, this depends on the 
uncertainty underlying in the model and on variation inherent in the allocation principles. 
Finally, present for both the impact category Land use and Water resource depletion is that 
the allocation principle determines whether or not the houses are within the assigned 
boundaries.  
==PAGE:22==
 
17 
The impact score relative to the allocated share of the Planetary Boundaries for all Mini CO2 
Houses and all examined impact categories using the PB-LCIA method are presented in 
Figure 4. AD House exceeds the assigned share of the Planetary Boundaries by an average of 
1900% across allocation principles for Climate change - energy imbalance. The house with 
the lowest potential impact, Q House, exceeds the boundaries by averagely 1200% across 
allocation principles for Climate change - energy imbalance, which is a considerable 
exceedance. Furthermore, the occupied SoSOS for the impact category Climate change - 
CO2 concentration is found to be somewhat similar to the occupied SoSOS for Climate 
change - energy imbalance. For the impact category Biogeochemical flows - N, the houses 
exceed the assigned share of the Planetary Boundaries, however for the impact category 
Land-system change - global, all houses is found to stay within the assigned share of the 
Planetary Boundaries irrespective of the uncertainty of the model and of the allocation 
principles. For the final impact category Freshwater use - global, it is found that the occupied 
SoSOS is highly dependent on the allocation principles and the uncertainty of the model. 
 
Since the presented results of the Base Scenario show that all houses exceed the assigned 
share of the carrying capacity and Planetary Boundary in most impact categories, it is 
relevant to investigate Scenario 6, which is the best case scenario. Thus, it is possible to state 
whether or not the impact potential of the houses in future scenarios will be low enough to be 
considered absolute sustainable. These results are presented in Figures 5 and 6. The results 
obtained for all scenarios can be seen in Supporting Information V and in Supporting 
Information I, Section III.I an analysis of how the houses perform across all scenarios can be 
found.

In general, the results for Scenario 6 obtained using the method CCnorm show that the Mini 
CO2 Houses exceed the assigned share of the carrying capacity, however contrary to the Base 
Scenario the conclusion depends highly on the uncertainty in the model and the choice of 
allocation principle. For the impact category Climate change, Ref House is found to be the 
house with the highest impact score exceeding the assigned share by averagely 250% across 
allocation principles, whereas IMF House is found to have the lowest impact score and thus 
the house that comes closest to staying within the assigned share of the carrying capacity by 
an average of 150% across allocation principles. As for the remaining impact categories, 
whether or not the houses impact potential stay within the assigned share of the carrying 
capacity depends on the uncertainty of the model and allocation principles.  
 
The impact potentials for Scenario 6 obtained using the method PB-LCIA, is found to exceed 
the assigned share of the Planetary Boundaries for all houses in many of the impact 
categories. Again the conclusion is found to be highly dependent on the embedded 
==PAGE:25==
 
20 
uncertainty of the model and of the allocation principles. For Climate change - energy 
imbalance and CO2 concentration, IMF House is the house, which exceeds the assigned 
share of the Planetary Boundaries the most by averagely 800% across allocation principles. 
For the same impact categories, the house closest to stay within the assigned share of the 
Planetary Boundaries is UP House with an average of 550% across allocation principles. For 
the impact category Biogeochemical flows - N and Freshwater use - global, it is found that all 
Mini CO2 Houses exceed the assigned share to some degree depending on the embedded 
uncertainty. Contrary, for the impact category Land-system change - global, all houses is 
found to stay within the assigned share of the Planetary Boundaries. 
 
Although it was not the goal of this assignment to compare the results obtained from the two 
methods, it is relevant to consider how the magnitude of the impact potentials rank between 
the methods. As described in Supporting Information I, Section IV.I a comparison between 
the methods can be justified if the focus of protection can be compared. Therefore, Table 4 
shows the range in which the occupied SoSOS can be found for the houses for impact 
categories related to climate change. It can be seen that for the method PB-LCIA up to 
5500% of the SoSOS is occupied if AD House is considered and allocation principle 1 is 
applied, while for the method CCnorm only up to 2150% of the SoSOS is occupied if AD 
House is considered and allocation principle 1 is applied.

Figure 7 show that allocation principle 1 allocate a 60% smaller SoSOS relative to allocation 
principle 2. Furthermore, it is found that allocation principle 2 and 4 provide similar results 
and likewise for allocation principle 3, 5 and 6. The difference between the allocation 
principle that allocate the lowest SoSOS (allocation principle 1) and the allocation principle, 
which allocate the greatest SoSOS (allocation principle 6), is found to be approx. 84%. 
Therefore, the choice of allocation principle is highly important and affects the results 
considerably.  
 
4 DISCUSSION  
4.1 Truly sustainable? 
For neither PB-LCIA nor CCnorm any house can be considered absolute sustainable in the 
Base Scenario, irrespective of which allocation principle is applied. In the Base Scenario, 
representing current conditions, no houses are within the SoSOS for impact categories related 
to climate change, indicating that even the best attempts at designing single family stand-
==PAGE:28==
 
23 
alone dwellings are insufficient. If Scenario 6 is considered, and CCnorm is applied as LCIA-
method it is possible that IMF House could be within the SoSOS for all impact categories and 
thereby absolute sustainable. However, this is only the case if the uncertainty of the model is 
taken into account. Contrarily, for PB-LCIA none of the houses can be considered absolute 
sustainable in Scenario 6, irrespective of the allocation principle and uncertainty of the 
model, thus calling for improvements in other parts of the modelled system. It should be 
noted that if a house is not within the SoSOS for all impact categories, it cannot be 
considered absolute sustainable, as the transgression of one Earth System process might push 
other Earth System processes towards reaching the threshold (Steffen et al., 2015). 
 
The results obtained in this study indicate that the architectural design of the houses has little 
impact on the houses’ ability to stay within the allocated SoSOS. From Section 3 it is evident 
that for the majority of the considered impact categories the houses either all stay within the 
SoSOS or all exceed the assigned share. Overall, consistency between the houses is seen for 
the impact categories Climate change and Land use for CCnorm and Climate change - energy 
imbalance, Climate change - CO2 concentration, Biogeochemical flows - N, and Land system 
change - global for PB-LCIA. In contrast, the impact categories Water resource depletion 
and Marine eutrophication for CCnorm and Freshwater use - global for PB-LCIA show a 
more varied performance of the houses.  
 
That being said, it is possible that the design of the houses can influence the environmental 
performance – hence there could be a direct link between environmental sustainability of 
buildings and building design. In this study it was found that UP House and Q House was 
consistently among the best performing houses. A common trait of the two houses is their 
size, UP House being the smallest (129 m2) and Q House only slightly larger (138 m2). 
Generally, it appears that the smallest houses (UP, Q and TMF) perform better than the larger 
houses (AD, IMF and Ref). In line with this, it was found that AD House, which is 
considerably larger than the other houses (160 m2), performs dramatically worse than e.g. UP 
House and Q House which is in line with the findings by Brejnrod et al. (2017) (for further 
details see Supporting Information I, Section IV.IV). Additionally, the superior performance 
of UP House and Q House indicate that choice of building components and operational 
energy- and water consumption has a notable influence on the environmental performance. 
The use of upcycled and recycled building components in UP House contribute to minimising 
both consumption of resources and energy for processing of raw materials. Similarly, the 
==PAGE:29==
 
24 
design of Q House, which encourages a reduced consumption of both energy and water 
during use, dramatically improves its environmental performance. This is supported by the 
sensitivity analysis, which found that the model was highly sensitive to changes of both 
parameters (for more details on the results of the sensitivity and uncertainty analysis, see 
Supporting Information I, Section III.IV). Furthermore, the results indicate that the structural 
system of wood in IMF House has a positive effect in climate change-related impact 
categories when impact assessment is done with ILCD 2011.  
 
4.2 Influence of methodology  
From the results presented in Section 3 it is evident that the choice of allocation principle has 
crucial influence on how large a SoSOS is occupied by the houses. Allocation principle 6 (i.e. 
utilitarian based by considering final consumption expenditure) allocate a greater SoSOS to 
the houses than the remaining allocation principles and thus more houses stay within the 
boundaries when this allocation principle is applied. This finding is in line with what has 
previously been established about the importance of the choice of allocation principle by both 
Sandin et al. (2015) and Ryberg et al. (2018). For the impact categories Land use, Water 
resource depletion, and Freshwater use - global, the choice of allocation principle determines 
whether or not a house is within the SoSOS. The definition of six varying allocation 
principles is an attempt at highlighting the uncertainty linked to the choice of allocation 
principle, but also an approach to a question without an answer - i.e. who has the right to 
impact the environment how much? It is a question, which cannot be answered without an 
ever-present bias depending on who replies.  
 
Sandin et al. (2015) considers the question as a matter of ethics, and attempts to answer the 
question by applying four common ethical principles. As has previously been argued by 
Brejnrod et al. (2017) there is no truly objective way to allocate the SOS to a building or any 
other service, as the allocation method always will be seen as more or less fair depending on 
the eyes of the beholder. While egalitarian approaches may seem fair given the implied 
equality of all individuals, it is not given that all people require the same share size of 
”ecological resource” to experience an equal degree of welfare (Ryberg, Owsianiak and 
Hauschild, 2018). As argued by Ryberg et al. (2018) people living in colder regions may 
require a larger SoSOS relative to people living in warmer regions because they would need 
energy for heating to experience the same degree of wellbeing. On the other hand, when 
considering allocation principles based on acquired rights there will be bias placed towards 
==PAGE:30==
 
25 
existing activities which currently has a high contribution to impacts, while activities that 
have a low contribution to impacts are assigned a smaller share (Ryberg, Owsianiak and 
Hauschild, 2018). 
 
Given the underlying theory of the two methods presented in Section 2.1, it was expected that 
when applying the PB-LCIA methodology a (relatively) larger SoSOS would be occupied by 
the houses, as the Planetary Boundaries are defined as “below” the carrying capacity of our 
planet (see Figure 1). In Section 3 the occupied SoSOS for impact categories related to 
climate change are considered for both methods and it can be seen that, as expected, the 
occupied SoSOS is substantially greater for the results obtained with PB-LCIA (see Table 4).  
However, as previously mentioned it is not possible to directly compare the results obtained 
with the two methods, due to a difference in Areas of Protection for the two methods 
(Ryberg, 2018). ILCD 2011 is concerned with the three Areas of Protection human health, 
natural environment and natural resources, whereas PB-LCIA only considers one - the 
Holocene-state of the Earth system in accordance with the Planetary Boundary framework 
(Ryberg et al., 2016).  
 
However, certain impact categories in PB-LCIA can - with some caution - be compared to 
those of ILCD 2011. Ryberg et al. (2018)(Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018) found 
that both Climate change - energy imbalance and Climate change - CO2 concentration (PB-
LCIA) had high coefficients of correlation with the impact category Climate change (ILCD 
2011). Others should preferably not be compared such as Land-system change - global (PB-
LCIA) and Land use (ILCD 2011) where low coefficients of correlation was found (Ryberg, 
Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018). For further details regarding the correlation of impact 
categories, see Supporting Information I, Section IV.I.  
 
4.3 Limitations and recommendations 
One of the most relevant limitations in the modelling of the studied systems is the service life 
of the Mini CO2 Houses. Assuming a realistic service life for building components as well as 
the entire building was one of the main challenges when the inventories were compiled. It 
was decided to strive for consistency in the modelling and therefore all assumptions were 
based on theoretical estimates of service life made by the Danish Building Research Institute 
(Aagaard et al., 2013). The uncertainty of this parameter is indisputable but as any estimation 
could be just as true as the next, it was found to be an acceptable assumption. Additionally, 
==PAGE:31==
 
26 
this parameter is not only related to a high level of uncertainty, it will similarly be subject to a 
high degree of variability. Based on this it should be considered if a more accurate approach 
to modelling the service life of the building could be adopted. One possible alternative 
approach is a study by Østergaard et al. (2018), where the service life of buildings is 
determined by the choice of cladding materials, location and use type. Applying this 
approach would have meant modelling an individually estimated service life of each Mini 
CO2 House. This could potentially have made the modelled systems a more accurate 
representation of reality, but in this study it was decided to focus on consistency in the 
modelling approach and therefore it seemed more appropriate to model the service life equal 
for all houses. For a more details on the assumed service life of the Mini CO2 Houses see 
Supporting Information I, Section IV.III. Generally, enabling the comparison between the 
Mini CO2 Houses was a main source of assumptions in this study. In Supporting Information 
I, Section IV.II this matter is discussed further.   
 
As described in previous Sections it is clear that the choice of allocation principle has a 
determining effect on how much of the SoSOS is occupied. This is a challenge, as it is 
inherently a matter of ethics and therefore problematic to apply when an objective conclusion 
is strived for. Furthermore, the allocation principles are continuously outdated as the world 
population is steadily growing, thereby constantly decreasing the SoSOS available to each 
individual. According to the UN, the world population will exceed 11 billion in 2100 (United 
Nations and Department of Economic and Social Affairs Population Division, 2017), which 
will decrease the size of allocation principle 1, 2 and 3 drastically, thus inspiring the need for 
even more ambitious strategies. 
 
Even though Section 4.2 found that the conclusions are overall robust against the choice of 
LCIA method, it is relevant to consider if the two applied methodologies are really 
comparable. One inherent difference is that PB-LCIA and CCnorm has different points of 
departure in their framework definition. In CCnorm carrying capacities are adapted as 
normalisation factors to comply with the impact categories of ILCD 2011, whereas in PB-
LCIA characterisation factors are adapted to fit with the Planetary Boundaries. However, this 
topic has not been thoroughly investigated yet and possibly more work is required in this 
area.  
 
==PAGE:32==
 
27 
It was found that the largest reduction in environmental impact compared to the Base 
Scenario was achieved in Scenario 1 and Scenario 3, i.e. avoiding an expansion of the house, 
and lowering the energy consumption by implementing smart technologies and introducing 
positive nudging to alter the residents’ behaviour. Thus, it can be argued that the most 
important factors towards achieving absolute sustainability are floor area per person and 
energy consumption per square meter. This is in line with the findings of similar studies 
(Ramesh, Prakash and Shukla, 2010; Sharma et al., 2011; Brejnrod et al., 2017).  
 
It is however important to note that from the results obtained in this study, achieving absolute 
sustainability for single family stand-alone dwellings still appear to be out of reach if current 
practices are not changed. The results call for drastic action in areas such as composition of 
energy grid mix, choice of materials, disposal routes and way of living. It was found that all 
parameters considered in this study, which covers a wide range of aspects, had a notably 
positive influence on the results, albeit some less than others. It is however clear that 
improving in only one area will very likely not be sufficient to achieve absolute 
sustainability. The need for improvement should not only be considered by architects and 
designers, but with equal urgency by politicians, industry stakeholder and consumers alike. It 
is essential that achieving absolute sustainability is considered a common goal for both 
industrial stakeholders, policy makers as well as private individuals/consumers.   
 
5 CONCLUSION 
This study has presented an absolute sustainability assessment of six single family stand-
alone dwellings using two methodologies to quantify absolute sustainability. It is found that 
neither of the assessed houses can be considered within the boundaries of absolute 
sustainability. AD House, i.e. flexible area, is found to be the worst performing house across 
all impact categories, scenarios and allocation principles, whereas Q and UP House, i.e. 
reduced energy consumption and recycling and reuse of materials respectively, is found to be 
the best. However, when accounting for uncertainty of the LCI, there is not a markedly 
difference in the impact potential for Q and UP house. Thus, it is difficult to conclude which 
sustainability strategy of the two that has the greatest potential to reach absolute 
sustainability. Furthermore, the conclusions are found to be overall independent of the 
assessment method applied, however highly dependent on the choice of allocation principle. 
Conclusively, the current approach to sustainable building design of single family stand-alone 
dwellings is not found to be ambitious enough to reach absolute sustainability and in order to 
==PAGE:33==
 
28 
be absolute sustainable according to the applied absolute environmental sustainability 
assessment methods, all sustainability influencing factors should improve considerably.

==PAGE:39==
 
 
3 
I 
Introduction 
This document is a continuation of the study on assessing the level of absolute sustainability 
of six single family stand-alone dwellings using the two methods carrying capacity-based 
normalisation factors (CCnorm) and Planetary Boundary-based Life Cycle Impact Assessment 
(PB-LCIA).  
 
Supporting Information I elaborates the underlying methodology and methodological 
choices applied in the study thereby facilitating transparency of the study. Furthermore, 
results, not possible to include in the Article due to restrictions in length, are presented, 
analysed and discussed, hence including aspects of the study not covered in the Article.  
 
Supporting Information II is a preliminary study for a project course at the Technical 
University of Denmark conducted prior to this study, presented in its unaltered form - i.e. as 
it was when it was originally handed in.  
 
In addition to the Article, Supporting Information I and Supporting Information II, three other 
documents (Supporting Information III, IV, V) containing background information and 
further details have been produced. These include lists of assumptions (Supporting 
Information III), the life cycle inventory for all houses (Supporting Information IV) and the 
results for all houses, scenarios, methods, impact categories and allocation principles 
(Supporting Information V). Supporting Information IV and V are attached as Excel sheets.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:40==
 
 
4 
II 
Methods and materials 
II.I 
Assessing absolute environmental sustainability 
Assessing absolute environmental sustainability requires two definitions; control variables 
and thresholds (Bjørn et al., 2016). Bjørn et al. (2016) define a control variable as “a 
numerical indicator of the structure and/or functioning of a natural system” and a threshold 
as “the maximum value of a control variable a natural system can withstand without 
experiencing negative changes in structure and/or functioning that are difficult or impossible 
to revert”. In both the Planetary Boundary and carrying capacity-framework control variables 
have been defined for the relevant Earth System processes. In the Planetary Boundary-
framework control variables are expressed through drivers, pressures, states, and impacts at 
both inventory, midpoint indicator, and endpoint indicator level. In Rockström et al. (2009) 
thresholds are defined as non-linear transitions in the functioning of coupled human-
environmental systems. A global threshold cannot be defined for all Earth System processes. 
In some cases, e.g. land-use change, a decline of key ecological functions may cause 
collapses in the system thereby generating feedbacks triggering or increasing the likelihood 
of crossing a global threshold in other processes (Rockström, W. Steffen, et al., 2009).  
  
In traditional LCAs environmental systems are represented as a linear function, where 
characterisation factors express change in impact per change in elementary flow (Ryberg, 
2018). However, when performing absolute sustainability assessments, the goal is to express 
absolute impact on the environment, where systems rarely exhibit a linear response function 
(Ryberg, 2018). Feedback mechanisms, both positive and negative, may lead to non-linear 
changes in the state of the environment and may push Earth Systems into alternative stable 
states (Scheffer and Carpenter, 2003; Ryberg, 2018).The critical transition from one stability 
domain to another when a threshold is crossed reflects an erosion of resilience of the Earth 
Systems (Lenton et al., 2008; Rockström, W. Steffen, et al., 2009). 
  
Boundaries – such as the Planetary Boundaries – differ from thresholds. They are values of 
the control variable determined by “us” defined at a distance from the threshold. This 
distance is defined by the willingness of the global community to accept a degree of risk, e.g. 
how close to a zone of uncertainty around a dangerous level or threshold do we dare go, 
and/or for how long can we temporarily transgress a boundary before a threshold is crossed ( 
(Rockström, W. Steffen, et al., 2009). The distance between the defined boundary and the 
actual threshold leaves room for a degree of uncertainty in the position of the threshold, but 
==PAGE:41==
 
 
5 
furthermore, it allows for timely action from society by the appearance of early warning signs 
(Rockström, W. Steffen, et al., 2009). 
 
II.II 
The Planetary Boundaries  
To assess absolute environmental sustainability it is necessary to define the size of the 
”room” below the boundary – i.e. the safe operating space (SOS). Ryberg et al. (2018) has 
defined the size of the SOS available for human activities as the value of the Planetary 
Boundary minus the ’natural background level’. The natural background level is the value of 
the control variable before human activities began affecting the Earth System process. Table 
1 shows the Planetary Boundaries (Rockström, W. Steffen, et al., 2009), the natural 
background level (Steffen et al., 2015) and available SOS (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et 
al., 2018).

Boundaries values and Natural background level (Steffen et al., 2015). The full safe 
operating space is used to calculate the available share of safe operating space for the 
houses.

II.III Carrying capacity-based normalisation factors 
The set of normalisation factors proposed by Bjørn and Hauschild (2015) express a person’s 
annual share of the carrying capacity. As described in the Article, these normalisation factors 
are not compliant with those of ILCD 2011 Midpoint+ (ILCD 2011), and has therefore been 
translated to be compliant with ILCD 2011 impact categories (Sala et al., 2016). Table 2 
shows the normalisation factors in their translated form.

II.IV Basis for calculating the share of safe operating space 
The first step of downscaling the Planetary Boundaries is to identify the competing activities 
as determined by Bjørn et al. (2016) and next be able to determine the share of safe operating 
space (SoSOS) available to one dwelling. Ryberg (2018) identified the frequency with which 
different allocation principles (e.g. utilitarian, egalitarian and acquired rights-based) have 
previously been applied. It is clear that an egalitarian approach is a common way of sharing 
the SOS among competing activities (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018). 
Furthermore, the review by Ryberg (2018) of studies, where a sharing of the SOS is done, 
finds that in country-scale studies it is common to apply a single stand-alone allocation 
principle, while a combination of two allocation principles is more common in sector and 
company scale studies (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Clavreul, et al., 2018). 
 
As described in the Article, six different allocation principles are applied in this study to test 
the robustness of our conclusion. In three out of six combinations we used an egalitarian 
approach (equal per capita) as a way of allocating a share to one person out of the global 
==PAGE:43==
 
 
7 
population. We then combined each of these three with a different second approach, two of 
these were utilitarian based, and one was acquired rights-based. In the latter case, where a 
acquired rights-based approach was applied, we assigned the share by considering current 
energy consumption of households.  
 
The two utilitarian approaches differ slightly. We considered the second step identified by 
Bjørn et al. (2016), which is to quantify the relative value of the studied activity or in other 
words what is the perceived value relative to the identified competing activities. When the 
SOS had been allocated to a per capita measure, we needed to identify the value of a dwelling 
to a person. We therefore needed to identify two levels of relative value - the value of a 
household to a person and the value of the dwelling out of the value of the household. A 
common way of quantifying value is to consider final consumption expenditure (FCE), i.e. 
how we spend our money. However, as a variation of this approach we argue a different way 
of quantifying the value of a household; time spent. How we spend our time is another way of 
expressing how we value elements of our life. Therefore, we estimated the number of hours 
spent at home each year as way of allocating a person’s share to household level. 
 
For two allocation principles we calculated the share allocated to the household by applying 
an acquired rights-principle by considering current CO2 emissions globally and for an 
average Danish household. We combined this with two approaches for calculating the share 
of the household allocated to the dwelling, FCE and energy use. Finally, we applied a strictly 
utilitarian approach by allocating a share to a dwelling by calculating the FCE related to a 
dwelling out of the current global FCE. How the allocation share is calculated according to 
the six allocation principles are shown in Equation 1 to 6.

Where Popworld is the world population and was at the time of this study 7.63 billion people 
(United Nations and Department of Economic and Social Affairs Population Division, 2017), 
FCEHH is the final consumption expenditure for a household (HH), FCEperson is the final 
consumption expenditure for a person, FCEdwe is the final consumption expenditure for a 
dwelling, Hhome is the hours spent at home, Hyear is the hours in a year, EHH is the energy 
consumption for an average household, Eperson is the energy consumption for one person, Edwe 
is the energy consumption for a dwelling, CO2HH is the CO2 emitted for an average 
household and CO2world is the CO2 emitted worldwide. For these calculations we applied a 
value of 3.61E13 kg CO2-eq for CO2world and 15,100 kg CO2-eq for CO2HH (The World 
Bank, 2014a, 2014b). Finally, N is defined as the number of persons in a household. The 
residents of the Mini CO2 Houses have for this study been assumed to be two adults, one 
teenager and one child. Since not all residents are adults, they cannot be assumed to have the 
same abilities in terms consumption and should therefore not be counted equally to adults. 
We adopt the scale defined by Frostell et al. (2015) and count the first adult as 1, the second 
adult as 0.6, the first child as 0.52, and the second child as 0.42 - a total of 2.54 residents or 
consumer units.  
 
Final Consumption Expenditure  
For allocation principle 1, 2, 5 and 6 we apply FCE as a way of quantifying the relative value 
of a dwelling. For allocation principle 1 we adopted the approach described by Brejnrod et al. 
(2017), where the share of the person-equivalent allocated to the household was found as the 
share of GDP that the final consumption expenditures of households represent (Brejnrod et 
al., 2017). Brejnrod et al. (2017) identified this share as 57.1% based on EU-28 conditions in 
2013. Next, the share of the household allocated to the dwelling service was based on the 
classification of individual consumption by purpose (COICOP). Brejnrod et al. (2017), 
identified two COICOP categories related to a building’s life cycle expenses; CP04 - 
Housing, water, electricity, gas, and other fuels and CP05 - Furnishings, household 
equipment, and routine household maintenance, and estimated the share of household 
==PAGE:45==
 
 
9 
consumption allocated to the dwelling service as 19.7%. For allocation principle 2 and 5 we 
only adopted the last step of the approach, i.e. allocating the share of the household to the 
dwelling. 
 
For allocation principle 6 we allocated the SOS solely by FCE. We found the share allocated 
to the dwelling by dividing the FCE of an average Danish household (Eurostat, 2018) by 
world FCE (The World Bank, 2016). Next, we applied the last step of the approach described 
by Brejnrod et al. (2017) to allocate a share of the household to the dwelling.  
 
Time spent 
For allocation principle 2 we applied time spent as an alternative way of quantifying value. 
The share of the person-equivalent allocated to the household was estimated by calculating a 
rough number of hours spent at home per year. We assumed that an average Dane works 7.4 
hours per day (37 hours per week), 252 days per year, and sleeps 8 hours per night. A year 
consists of 8760 hours, and based on the aforementioned assumptions we spend 1864.8 hours 
at work and 2920.0 hours in bed. This leaves 3975.2 awake hours where we are not at work. 
For the sake of simplicity we assumed that half of these are spent at home relaxing, cooking, 
and cleaning, while the other half is spent outside of home travelling, exercising, and 
socialising. Adding this half to the number of hours spent sleeping gives a total of 4907.6 
hours or 56% of the total number of hours in a year spent at home.  
 
Energy consumption 
For allocation principle 3 and 4 we applied energy consumption as a way of allocating the 
SOS according to an acquired rights-principle. Based on data from Eurostat (Eurostat, 2016) 
we found that the total energy consumption of Sweden is 2.06E12 MJ, and divided this by the 
current population of Sweden (9.99 million people) to calculate the energy consumption per 
capita, Eperson. We based the allocation on data from the web-based environmental feedback 
tool EcoRunner, which is designed for Swedish households aiming at increasing the 
awareness of consumers (Frostell et al., 2015). EcoRunner has been designed to fit with the 
COICOP categories and divides household consumption into four main purpose categories; 
home and living - MP1, household purchases - MP2, leisure - MP3, and transports - MP4. It 
was assumed that the sum of all MPs corresponds to the total household energy consumption, 
EHH. The tool calculates the energy use associated with the consumption of products or 
services at household level. Dividing the total household energy consumption, EHH, from 
==PAGE:46==
 
 
10 
EcoRunner with energy consumption per capita, Eperson, gives the share of the person 
equivalent allocated to the household. We calculated this to be 49.9%.  
 
When calculating the share of the household allocated to the dwelling service we found that 
only Main Purpose 1 was relevant when considering energy use during a building life cycle. 
We calculated the share of energy consumption associated with Main Purpose 1 out of the 
total household energy consumption, i.e. the sum of energy consumption associated with 
Main Purpose 1-4. We found this share to be 56.2%. However, not all subcategories within 
Main Purpose 1 were found related to the energy consumption of the dwelling service. Table 
3 shows the share of subcategories that the energy consumption of the dwelling service is 
related to.
