II.V 
Mini CO2 House project 
In 2011 the Danish association Realdania initiated a project with the goal of investigating 
how CO2 emissions linked to the built environment can be reduced. From 2013 to 2014 five 
different houses were built as a part of this project. The houses were all single family stand-
alone dwellings with floor areas between 129 and 160 m2. Each house seeks to reduce CO2 
emissions linked to buildings and the built environment in different ways. In the following 
paragraphs the concept and key characteristics of each house will be introduced. 
 
Upcycle House  
Upcycle House (UP House) focuses on how recycling by adding value can save resources and 
decrease CO2 emissions during construction of buildings. Upcycling means that a product 
categorised as waste is reused in an optimal and refined manner (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 
2013b). The structural system of UP House is two reclaimed shipping containers. The 
containers have been placed at a distance apart to make the floor plan of the house more or 
less a square. The foundation consists of helical piers made of reclaimed steel. An old ice rink 
==PAGE:48==
 
 
12 
has been cut up to form the footing of the building and at the same time conceals the 
insulation; shredded styrofoam waste. The benefit of using helical piers is the fact that once 
the building is ready to be demolished the piers can easily be moved and reused in another 
location, thereby minimising use of scarce resources and CO2 emissions during production of 
new steel. The walls are insulated with paper wool made from Danish newspapers. The 
external cladding is corrugated aluminium sheets made from recycled soda cans while the 
internal cladding is gypsum boards containing 25% recycled gypsum and glue-free OSB 
boards. Internal flooring is slices of bottle corks, glue free OSB boards made from wood 
waste from furniture production and reclaimed bricks (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2013b). 
 
The Innovative Maintenance-free House  
The Innovative Maintenance-free House (IMF House) excels in two areas; maintenance and 
service life. It differs from the Traditional Maintenance-free House (TMF House) by the 
choice of materials. TMF House (described in the next paragraph) is built from traditional 
materials that are known to have a long service life, while IMF House is built from new 
materials that are expected to have a long service life (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014b). IMF 
House has a theoretical service life of 150 years and a building envelope that does not require 
any maintenance over the first 50 years of use (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014b). IMF House 
consists of 18 prefabricated wood elements assembled at a factory and later moved by truck 
to the building site. The choice of prefabricated elements has given IMF House several 
qualities that help ensure its long service life. The wood elements were assembled in a dry 
setting, which helped avoid condensation that could damage the structural system over time. 
Furthermore, the setting allowed a high level of precision which has ensured a quality of the 
assembled elements that could not have been achieved had the elements been assembled on-
site (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014b). Once finished, the elements can be assembled to form 
the house over the course of a few days. Generally, the prefabricated elements of IMF House 
help minimise waste and save valuable time during the construction process (Realdania Byg 
and Kleis, 2014b). 
 
IMF House is supported by a pile foundation that not only ensures good ventilation around 
the wooden structure, but also helps bring down the CO2 emissions from concrete 
production, as less concrete is required for pile foundations than for solid slab foundations. 
To protect the vulnerable wooden construction the entire building is layered with tiles made 
from recycled glass. Glass has a theoretically infinite service life, and will almost certainly 
==PAGE:49==
 
 
13 
outlive the building by several hundred years (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014b). The glass 
cladding has therefore been designed to be disassembled and reused in another location once 
IMF House is demolished. The cladding is offset 50 millimetres from the structural frame, 
and similar to the pile foundation this space helps ensure good ventilation of the building 
envelope. Potentially vulnerable elements, e.g. doors and windows, have been extruded back 
into the facade which provides them with an overhang that shields from rain and wind 
(Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014b). IMF House has no drain pipes or roof gutters, as these 
often require maintenance and replacements. Instead, the house is surrounded by a 
stormwater trench with inlaid gabions, which ensures that the risk of water damages to the 
structural system is minimised (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014b). 
  
The interior of IMF House is also focused on minimising the need for maintenance. Very few 
surfaces require repainting over time and generally materials that gain patina have been 
chosen over materials that will show unflattering signs of wear and tear. Finally, an important 
factor was the need for a timeless design and a high level of flexibility in the way the house 
can be used. This has been ensured by a minimalistic interior and the possibility to add 
interior walls if need be (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014b). 
  
The Traditional Maintenance-free House  
In this house the focus is on lowering maintenance and improving the service life using 
traditional materials that throughout centuries have demonstrated a long service life. The 
Traditional Maintenance-free House (TMF House) is designed to have a service life of at 
least 150 years, which is double or triple of a typical single-family house in Denmark 
(Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014a). In order to obtain this long service life the building design 
is based on what have been previously experienced on family houses located in Denmark. It 
is found that damages often occur where different kinds of materials are combined since 
materials move differently relative to each other (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014a). Hence, 
the focus when designing and constructing the TMF House have been to use the same kind of 
materials and thereby avoiding damages, resulting in a maintenance-free period of 50 years 
(Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014a). The building envelope is constructed of brickwork on the 
outside and blocks of brickwork, consisting of many cavities providing insulating properties, 
on the inside. By using these blocks of brickwork, classic types of insulation and the vapour 
barrier can be spared, resulting in a building envelope consisting of primarily one material, 
brickwork, which is quite simple to construct (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014a). As a side 
==PAGE:50==
 
 
14 
benefit, saving the insulation and the vapour barrier results in an healthy indoor climate 
improving the quality of life for the residents (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014a). Since 
cellulose fibres is well suited to transport humidity in the air, using this type of insulation 
obviates the use of a vapour barrier (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014a). 
  
In addition to the choice of material, the architectural design of the house is of great 
importance when trying to reduce the maintenance and improving the service life. For 
instance, the roof is designed to have a high pitch to eliminate water efficiently and reduce 
damages as well as having a wide overhang, which protects the building envelope from the 
weather. Moreover, the wide overhang prevents superheating in the summer, while still 
allowing sunlight in in the winter (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014a).  
  
The Adaptable House  
The concept of the Adaptable House (AD House) is flexibility and variability during its 
service life. This focus arises from the changing needs and requirements a typical Danish 
family will experience during a lifetime - couples move in together and have children, the 
children move out of home, couples get divorced and so on (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 
2013a). Studies show that when Danish families decide to move, two out of three Danish 
families move to a new home within the same municipality (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 
2013a). In other words, they do not move because of the location, but because of the 
functionalities of the house (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2013a). To cope with this issue, AD 
House is designed to accommodate the changing needs and to be adaptable during the service 
life. This is, among others, achieved by constructing the house of components that are 
possible to disassembly, providing the opportunity to expand the house without destroying 
existing and remaining components. Another detail that provides flexibility is that the 
exterior walls are the only ones that are load bearing. This enables the interior walls to be 
moved freely to create one, two, three or four rooms. In addition, the kitchen is constructed in 
a way, which facilitates different layouts of the floor plan. By installing sliding doors it is 
possible to shut off or open up to the adjoining rooms, the living room and the dining room, 
as desired (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2013a). If it, after all, turns out that the house does not 
fulfil the residents need, the AD House is prepared for an extension in ground floor and first 
floor by having a overhang and a balcony corresponding to the space of one room (Realdania 
Byg and Kleis, 2013a). By incorporating the changing needs into the design of the house, 
==PAGE:51==
 
 
15 
reconstructions and extensions can be avoided and the consumption of time, resources, 
materials, and hence CO2, can be reduced (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2013a). 
  
The Quota House  
The concept of the Quota House (Q House) is to focus on the behaviour of the residents and 
their consumption patterns, since these are important parameters when considering the 
environmental impact of a house. To help the residents change their consumption patterns 
towards a more sustainable way of living, the house is designed with different features. One 
feature is innovative technologies integrated in the house which monitors the consumption of 
electricity, water and heat, hence making the consumption pattern transparent (Realdania Byg 
and Kleis, 2014c). The consumption is measured against a quota, defined by the residents of 
the house, that sets an ambitious target for the amount of CO2 “allowed” to be emitted each 
month (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014c). To support the residents towards a more 
sustainable consumption pattern, a screen is installed in the central zone of the house making 
it easy and straightforward to read off information about the consumption pattern and 
compare this to the quota of the day or month (Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014c). If the quota 
is exceeded the house shuts down and a button placed beneath the screen allows the residents 
to activate the systems in the house again, increasing the residents’ awareness of the energy 
use.   
 
Finally, the architecture of the house also helps the residents to lower the consumption. The 
different functionalities of the house such as the kitchen, bedrooms and living room are 
carefully placed in respect to daylight, orientation of the sun and the need for privacy. In 
addition, features such as a covered outdoor space for drying clothes to save tumble drying 
and a pantry to save space in the refrigerator is integrated. These functionalities are possible 
to integrate in single-family dwellings and can help reduce the environmental impact 
(Realdania Byg and Kleis, 2014c). 
 
II.VI Defining the scenarios 
The six houses all focus on different sustainability initiatives in the building sector and to test 
which trend is the most effective way to reduce the environmental impact, six scenarios 
containing parameters that are likely to change in the future, such as energy consumption, 
disposal routes and energy mix, have been developed. All scenarios are derived from the 
Base Scenario, which represents the building industry and infrastructure as it is today. 
==PAGE:52==
 
 
16 
Scenario 1 addresses the need for expanding a house if the number of residents increases. 
According to a report by SPREAD Sustainable Lifestyles (Backhaus et al., 2011) one of the 
major trends in the European Union is shrinking household sizes and one of the opportunities 
for a more sustainable way of living is smaller living spaces, as more dense living can 
support a sustainable lifestyle more efficiently (Backhaus et al., 2011). If we assume that we 
as consumers in the future will be more aware of how our lifestyle choices impact the 
environment there is a good chance that people will choose a more compact way of living and 
avoid expanding their homes. We have therefore modelled a scenario where none of the 
houses are expanded during their lifetime.  
 
In Scenario 2 it is assumed that consumption of energy for heating, electricity, and water will 
decrease in the future. The trend shows that the focus on environmental aspects is growing in 
all aspects of our life and this naturally reflects in our way of living (Backhaus et al., 2011). 
According to a survey conducted by Eurobarometer, people in the EU-27 are increasingly 
seeking to reduce their energy and water consumption to approach a more sustainable way of 
living (Backhaus et al., 2011). Furthermore, buildings are designed to be more energy 
efficient and effective by introducing high insulation standards, airtight building envelopes 
and automatic ventilation with heat recovery (Backhaus et al., 2011). Thus, we have 
modelled Scenario 2 to reflect the trend of users and technology changing towards 
sustainable living. Scenario 3 addresses the development towards a greater share of 
renewable energy. Constantly, new technological innovations within the energy sector are 
developed in the effort to increase energy efficiency and support a sustainable development 
(Backhaus et al., 2011). Besides the energy sector developing towards a more sustainable 
pathway, local renewable energy technologies are installed in more and more small-scale 
households and businesses (Backhaus et al., 2011). To accommodate this development, 
Scenario 3 is modelled with a energy grid mix reflecting future conditions.  
 
In general, the focus on sustainable development within all aspects of the building sector is 
increasing. Scenario 4 addresses the inevitable aspect of the materials used for a building. 
Along with more and more people being concerned about saving resources (Backhaus et al., 
2011), the focus on using materials to save resources have increased. As the report by 
SPREAD Sustainable Lifestyles state: “...there is a growing awareness of the need for 
change and a growing willingness to invest in environmentally friendly materials.” 
(Backhaus et al., 2011). Architects are taught in innovative sustainable building design using 
==PAGE:53==
 
 
17 
recyclable materials with low maintenance and long service life and the concept of nearly-
zero or positive energy balance buildings are aimed for (Backhaus et al., 2011). As Scenario 
4 reflects this trend by prolonging the service life of the materials, Scenario 5 focuses on the 
materials and the disposal of these in the End of Life stage. One of the upcoming initiatives to 
reduce the environmental concerns regarding building materials is circular economy. The 
concept of circular economy is the idea of closed loops, where repair, reuse, remanufacturing, 
refurbishing and recycling are key aspects (Geissdoerfer et al., 2017) and thus closely linked 
to the disposal of materials. Scenario 5 reflects this trend by allowing for a greater share of 
materials recycled. Finally, as previously mentioned Scenario 6 combines Scenario 1 to 5 and 
reflects a best case scenario if all future trends are taken into account.  
 
II.VII Basis for the LCAs 
The following paragraphs present a more detailed version of the LCAs of this study. The 
paragraphs follow the overall structure of ISO 14040:2006, ISO 14044:2006 and DS/EN 
15978:2011 (Dansk Standard, 2006, 2008, 2012) covering the key elements of Goal and 
scope definition and Life cycle inventory.  
 
  
 
 
The LCA performed in this study is intended to assess the level of sustainability in the built 
environment. The LCA results will be used to determine if current building practices can be 
considered absolute sustainable. Furthermore, a comparison between different design 
strategies will be made to determine which strategy is the most efficient towards achieving 
sustainable single family stand-alone dwellings. The results of this study will be publicly 
disclosed as a step towards enlightening both the public, industry stakeholders and politicians 
on the importance of change in our current practices. The study is a part of a Master’s thesis 
at the Technical University of Denmark. All LCI data was supplied and originally collected 
by the Danish Building Research Institute. 
 
II.VII.I Scope definition 
The LCAs carried out in this study all have a cradle-to-grave scope. All life cycle stages are 
included and both LCI results and an impact assessment are presented.  
 
Reference flow 
As stated in the Article, the functional unit (FU) is defined as ”Annually house one family in 
a stand-alone dwelling in Denmark” and the reference flow necessary to fulfil the FU is 
==PAGE:54==
 
 
18 
assumed to be one Mini CO2 House. As can be seen from Table 4 the houses differ in terms 
of floor area and service life. However, as the FU is defined per year and all elementary flows 
therefore have been calculated to fit this, it is not necessary (nor mathematically relevant) to 
scale the service life of any of the houses to make them comparable.  
 
Table 4 - All Mini CO2 Houses and the respective areas as well as assumed service life. 
(Ref – Reference House, UP – Upcycle House, TMF – Traditional Maintenance-free House, 
IMF – Innovative Maintenance-free House, AD – Adaptable House, Q – Quota House)

LCI modelling and handling of multifunctional processes 
All multifunctional processes were handled by system expansion. However, recycling was 
modelled as cut-off and therefore no crediting from these processes are included. For all 
cases of recycling the necessary processes for preparing a product for the next life cycle 
system were included in this system, e.g. remelting of steel or plastic. For UP House the main 
part of the building components is reused or reclaimed products. These were modelled as 
having zero impact in the Product stage. A full overview of how building components in UP 
House were modelled can be found in Supporting Information III, Table 8.  
 
System boundaries 
Although this study is of a comparative nature it was not possible to exclude any processes 
even though they were modelled equally for all houses. As this is a study of absolute 
sustainability it is necessary to include all processes contributing to the total environmental 
impact of the system in order to achieve a true measure of how big a SoSOS is occupied by 
one building.  
==PAGE:55==
 
 
19 
 
Figure 1 - System boundaries for all modelled systems.  
 
As can be seen from Figure 1 the foreground system for all houses include all processes that 
was modelled based on primary data from the Danish Building Research Institute. The 
background system was modelled in SimaPro version 8.0 (SimaPro) (PRé, 2019) using 
average processes from the database ecoinvent version 3.4 (ecoinvent) (Ecoinvent, 2019).  
 
Geographical, time and technological scopes 
All modelled systems are located in Nyborg, Denmark. The LCI data used to model the 
foreground system is representative for a Danish context, and parameters such as energy mix, 
==PAGE:56==
 
 
20 
energy consumption, and disposal routes were all modelled according to current conditions in 
Denmark. The background system was modelled using average processes and represents a 
global context. The LCI data supplied by the Danish Building Research Institute was 
collected before the completion of the buildings around 2012-2013 and can reasonably be 
considered representative for current practices. Given the nature of absolute environmental 
sustainability assessments it is not problematic that the LCAs of this study reflect current 
practices, even though the service life of the houses extends 120-150 years into the future. 
While only one year is considered, it is assumed that the elementary flows from all life cycles 
associated with continuously fulfilling the functional unit will occur in an “average” year. 
This means that projecting the development of e.g. technologies or consumption into the 
future is not required, and should instead be handled by considering “what if”-scenarios 
where an average future year is considered (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018).  
 
 
 
 
 
 
II.VII.II Life Cycle Inventory 
The different life cycle stages and the inherent assumptions made for these stages are 
presented in the following paragraphs. A comprehensive list of assumptions can be found in 
Supporting Information III, Table 1 to 9. 
 
Product stage 
The data provided by the Danish Building Research Institute are extensive and covers all 
building components for each Mini CO2 House. To model the inventories in SimaPro 
assumptions had to be made. Certain building components were given as a trademarked 
product and in these cases a unique component was modelled based on an EPD. Furthermore, 
it was not possible to find representative products in the ecoinvent database for all building 
components. In these cases, an assumption was made regarding a suitable alternative. A full 
list of how all building components were modelled in SimaPro can be found in Supporting 
Information III, Table 3 to 6. Further details regarding the modelling of the inventories can be 
found in Section II.VII.II.  
 
For UP House several of the building components are either “upcycled” - i.e. reclaimed waste 
- or recycled. All upcycled components were modelled as having zero impact. For recycled 
products where information on recycled content were available (e.g. EPDs) a customised 
process in SimaPro was created and assumed to be representative. If no details exist on the 
==PAGE:57==
 
 
21 
content or composition of the product they were modelled as the most common not-recycled 
alternative. For further information on this, see Supporting Information III, Table 3 to 6.  
 
 
 
 
 
Construction stage 
It was assumed that the construction of the houses will lead to some waste of the building 
components. This was accounted for in the Construction stage and a waste share between 0% 
and 10% was assumed for all components (Bossink and Brouwers, 1996). A list of assumed 
waste shares for all components can be found in Supporting Information III, Table 2. 
Furthermore, it was assumed that an amount of energy is needed to construct the houses. The 
amount of energy needed per square meter was based on a study by Cole (Cole, 1998). This 
study offers an estimated average for three types of structures; wood, steel and concrete. 
Based on the characteristics of the building components, each Mini CO2 House was 
classified as either a wooden structure, steel structure or concrete structure. These are very 
rough assumptions, as all houses contain more than one type of structure (e.g. concrete 
foundation, wooden structural system). Additionally, there is a risk that the values are 
outdated, as the study was published in 1998 and it is likely that technologies related to 
building construction have evolved since then. Therefore, the models were tested for 
sensitivity towards this parameter. More information on this can be found in Section III.IV.  
 
Table 5 - Amount of energy used for construction of one m2 house for each type of structural 
system. Each house is categorised below its type of structural system. 
(Ref – Reference House, UP – Upcycle House, TMF – Traditional Maintenance-free House, 
IMF – Innovative Maintenance-free House, AD – Adaptable House, Q – Quota House)

Use stage 
According to DS/EN 15978:2011 only energy use related to operation of a building should be 
included in building LCAs - i.e. heating, domestic hot water supply, air conditioning, 
ventilation, lighting, and auxiliary energy used for pumps, control and automation (Dansk 
==PAGE:58==
 
 
22 
Standard, 2012). Therefore, energy use for all appliances that are not building related were 
excluded in the Use stage.  
 
The Use stage for all Mini CO2 Houses was based on report by the Alexandra Institute and 
MOE A/S (Rasmussen and Johannsen, 2016), in which the Q House is evaluated two years 
following finalisation of the project. The report presents electricity, water, heating, and 
domestic hot water readings for a year and compares these numbers with the estimated values 
for Q House and a standard house. Table 6 shows both the estimated and recorded values 
modified to fit with the scope of this study. The current residents of Q House is a couple with 
no children (Rasmussen and Johannsen, 2016) and it was therefore assumed that both their 
water and domestic hot water-consumption is half of what the family of four in this study 
would consume (hence, all water and domestic hot water-values have been multiplied by 
two). Additionally, only the contribution from lighting was included in the electricity 
consumption, omitting appliances and equipment as recommended by DS/EN 15978:2011. 
According to the report by the Alexandra Institute and MOE A/S lighting is estimated to 
account for 12% of the total electricity consumption (hence, only 12% of the total electricity 
consumption is reported in Table 6) (Rasmussen and Johannsen, 2016). 
 
Table 6 - The electricity, water, heat and district hot water consumption estimated and 
recorded for a standard house and the Quota House (Rasmussen and Johannsen, 2016)

Even though the recorded consumption data for Q House are considered more realistic than 
the estimated values, all Mini CO2 Houses were modelled with estimated energy and water 
consumption data. The reason behind this choice was that no recorded data exists for other 
houses than Q House, and comparing estimated data with recorded data would compromise 
the comparison of the systems, as ISO 14044 dictates that if systems are compared the data 
quality should be equally comparable (Dansk Standard, 2008). Therefore, as a result of 
==PAGE:59==
 
 
23 
shortcomings in terms of data availability, the energy and water consumption of all houses 
other than Q House was modelled as the standard house from Table 6. Energy for heating and 
lighting was assumed to be dependent on the floor area of each house.  
 
In the Base Scenario the energy grid mix was modelled as current conditions, by considering 
data from the Danish Energy Agency (Energistyrelsen, 2018b). Table 7 shows the shares of 
energy sources in 2018 and projected towards 2030 and shares of renewable energy for both 
years.

In SimaPro a process consisting of these shares of energy was modelled by assuming 
representative processes for each source of energy. A list of these can be found in Supporting 
Information III, Table 6.   
 
When considering Table 7 it is noteworthy that the share of renewable energy only increases 
with 0.76 pct. points from 2018 to 2030. The Danish Energy Agency predicts that the share 
of renewable energy will increase substantially until 2021, but after this point the share will 
decline towards 2030 (Energistyrelsen, 2018a). This decline can be explained by a substantial 
increase in energy consumption in the coming years combined with a lack of new renewable 
energy initiatives. The progress towards 2021 is driven by the construction of new wind 
farms both on- and offshore and a growing share of biomass energy. The increase in energy 
consumption is among others caused by an increasing demand from data centres in the 
coming years (Energistyrelsen, 2018a). Thus, the stagnating development of new initiatives 
==PAGE:60==
 
 
24 
combined with an increasing consumption will only lead to a small increase in the share of 
renewable energy.  
 
The district heating mix was similarly modelled as current conditions by considering data 
from the Danish Energy Agency (Energistyrelsen, 2018a). Table 8 shows the shares of 
district heating sources in 2018 and projected towards 2030. In SimaPro a process consisting 
of these shares of district heating sources was modelled by assuming representative processes 
for each source of heat. A list of these can be found in Supporting Information III, Table 7.

The service life of the individual building components were assumed according to 
estimations made by the Danish Building Research Institute (Aagaard et al., 2013). It was 
decided to model service lives as actual service life and a list of the assumed service lives can 
be found in Supporting Information III, Table 1 and IV. If the reference flow of a building 
component, needed to fulfil the FU, is greater than one - i.e. if the service life of a building 
component is shorter than the service life of the entire building - it was modelled as being 
replaced if it is accessible without disassembling other building components. This assumption 
was based on the fact that building components that are exposed to the environment (both 
indoor and outdoor) will be worn more and will therefore need replacement more often, while 
components that are protected in the structural system will not be as damaged by time. 
Furthermore, it was decided to be unlikely that replacements will be made in e.g. a 
foundation even though some building components have a service life shorter than the entire 
building. In accordance with DS/EN 15798 only a full number of replacements were 
modelled. In cases where a partial number of replacements were needed to cover the entire 
service life of the houses, the value obtained was rounded up (Dansk Standard, 2012).  
 
 
 
==PAGE:61==
 
 
25 
Expansion 
The AD House was designed as flexible and, among other things, easy to expand if the 
residents suddenly require more space. Additionally, the expansion was designed in a way so 
that it is easy to disassemble, if the needs of the residents change and they can spare the extra 
space. The idea of this design is to minimise the amount of square meters and thereby reduce 
the need for heating and cooling. In other words, an expansion of this house is a key ability of 
the AD House. Therefore, if the houses were to be considered comparable it was necessary to 
compensate for this ability in all other models. For more details on this matter, see Section 
IV.II. As a consequence, an expansion of all houses were modelled. The expansion of the AD 
House is 55 m2 and the equivalent amount of building components to each of the other houses 
were therefore added, i.e. if considering Q House an extra 40.4% (55 m2 out of 136 m2) of all 
components were added to represent an extension of the original building.  
 
To assess the need for an expansion, changes in family size was assumed. It was assumed that 
a couple move into a house and after seven years the family has grown to include two 
children bringing the total number of residents to four persons. Next, it was assumed that the 
children live at home for 20 years and after this, the couple live in the house for an additional 
10 years without any children. If this cycle is repeated for 120 years, it amounts to four 
periods of seven years with two persons, three periods of 20 years with four persons, three 
periods of 10 years with two persons and one period of 2 years with 4 persons. The weighted 
average of residents over 120 years was therefore 3.03 residents. This meant that the 
consumption of water for operation and maintenance had to be modified with a factor 0.758 
(3.03 (weighted number of residents) out of 4 (maximum number of residents)) to account for 
the changing number of residents. It was only necessary to apply this factor to water 
consumption, as it was assumed that the need for heating and lighting would not be affected 
by the number of residents. 
 
It was assumed that the expansion added to AD House was designed and intended to be 
removed when the family size decreases. For all other houses, it was assumed that the 
expansion is a standard annex, which was assumed to be too inconvenient to remove once 
built. This means that when the first family living in the house adds an expansion, it remains 
for the entire service life of the building. As a result, the first seven years of the building’s 
service life there is no expansion, while for the last 113 years (143 years for IMF and TMF 
due to a service life of 150 years) there is an expansion, adding 55 m2 to the floor area (and 
==PAGE:62==
 
 
26 
thereby increasing the need for heating and lighting). This should give the AD House the 
intended benefit of shaping itself to the needs of the family and minimising the amount of 
square meters per person.    
 
End of Life 
All building components were considered waste at the end of the buildings’ life cycle and 
processed as such. Waste processing was modelled and the elementary flows for waste can be 
seen in the inventories in Supporting Information IV. As indicated by DS/EN 15798, all 
environmental loads (e.g. emissions) from waste disposal are considered part of the building 
life cycle, according to the “polluter pays” principle (Dansk Standard, 2012). However, as the 
systems were modelled with a cut-off principal, all potential benefits from utilisation of 
recycled in the next product system were not accounted for as crediting in this study. Waste 
fractions (incineration, landfill, recycling) were assumed for all building components and a 
complete list can be found in Supporting Information III, Table 2 and Supporting Information 
IV. It was assumed that the same amount of water consumed in the life cycle stage Use is 
treated as wastewater and was modelled as such.  
 
Transportation 
No transportation was modelled for any systems. All material-inputs were modelled as 
‘market for’-processes and therefore already include average transportation processes for the 
chosen geographical coverage.  
 
Basis for sensitivity and uncertainty analysis 
The aim of the sensitivity and uncertainty analysis is to identify the parameters that 
potentially have an influence on the LCIA results. These were identified as amount of 
building materials, energy amount for construction, energy amounts for use, service life of 
the building, service life of building materials, area of the houses, expansion/no expansion, 
energy mix, End of Life routes, choice of processes in model.  
 
In this study we applied an approach where we first seek to determine the model parameters 
that can be considered uncertain. If a model parameter is found to be uncertain we next 
investigated the sensitivity of said parameter. We considered uncertainty by applying both a 
quantitative and qualitative approach. In Table 9 we discuss the uncertainty of each model 
parameter qualitatively.  
==PAGE:63==
 
 
27 
Table 9 - Qualitative uncertainty analysis of the parameters of the inventory model and 
justification of why each parameter is considered uncertain. 
Parameter 
Uncertain? Comment 
Building material 
amounts 
No 
It was assumed that all data points of our model must have the same 
degree of uncertainty, as they were collected by one person over a limited 
period of time. Therefore, the approach and level of thoroughness with 
which the data was collected were assumed to be identical for all houses 
and all building components. Generally, the authors of this study have no 
reason to suspect that any particular material value has been reported or 
assumed wrongly.  
Energy consumption 
for construction 
Yes 
The amount of energy modelled for the construction phase of the LCA 
was based on a study by Cole (1998) where the amount of energy required 
for different types of structural types (wood, steel, concrete) was reported. 
While this source serves the purpose of providing an estimate of required 
energy for constructions quite well, the numbers provided in the study are 
very likely outdated as quite a bit of technological development can be 
said to have happened since 1998.  
Energy and water 
consumption for use 
Yes 
The modelled energy and water consumption for operation and 
maintenance of the houses was based on estimates made by experts 
retrieved from a report by Alexandra Institute and MOE (Rasmussen and 
Johannsen, 2016). As the modelled energy and water use does not reflect 
actual consumption data for the specific houses, the parameter was 
considered uncertain.  
Service life of 
building 
Yes 
The modelled service life of the buildings was highly uncertain. We chose 
to model an estimated service life of 120 years (150 years for IMF and 
TMF House), which reflects the suggestions made by the Danish Building 
Research Institute (Aagaard et al., 2013), but the “real” service life of a 
building might be affected by costs, aesthetics, and technical 
requirements.  
Area of houses 
No 
The actual areas of the houses are known, and were therefore not 
considered uncertain.  
Expansion/no 
expansion 
Yes 
As it was not possible to predict whether or not the houses are expanded 
during its service life, this parameter was considered highly uncertain.  
Energy mix 
Yes 
As previously described, the modelled energy mix for 2018 and 2030 was 
based on data from the Danish Energy Agency and thus considered 
representative for the case of today. However, as the energy mix during 
the service life of the houses was predicted to change in the future, the 
parameter was considered uncertain when considering the service life of 
the houses.  
Service life of 
building materials 
Yes 
The service life of the materials was based on the actual technical service 
life estimated by the Danish Building Research Institute (Aagaard et al., 
2013). Although this was considered expert knowledge, the parameter 
was considered uncertain due to the fact that it is not the “real” service 
life of the specific materials. 
End of Life routes 
Yes 
The End of Life routes were modelled based on literature reviews and 
expert judgement by the Danish Building Research Institute. However, as 
the specific End of Life routes for the materials were to some extent 
unknown, the parameter was considered uncertain.  
==PAGE:64==
 
 
28 
Choice of processes 
in model 
Yes 
The processes chosen in the model were based on literature such as EPD’s 
(Institut Bauen und Umwelt e.V., 2014, 2017b, 2017a; Earthsure, 2017; 
EPD Danmark, 2017; Bre Global Verified EPD, 2018), likely alternatives 
and the researchers best knowledge. As the choice of processes was 
restricted by the use of database, some assumptions were made and 
therefore the processes were considered an uncertain parameter.  
 
To test the uncertainty of the model, we performed a quantitative uncertainty analysis in 
SimaPro using Monte Carlo simulations. A lognormal distribution was assumed for two 
parameters, namely water use and energy for heating and the geometric standard deviation 
(GSD) were calculated using the numbers from Table 10.  
 
Table 10 - Average consumption of water and district heating valid for a typical Danish 
household. These were used to calculate the geometric standard deviation for the Monte 
Carlo analysis (Dansk vand og Spildevandsforening and DTU Miljø & Ressourcer, 2003; 
Gram-Hanssen and Rhiger Hansen, 2016)

Besides testing the model against uncertainty for the two parameters, a Monte Carlo analysis 
automatically includes the embedded uncertainty of all inventory data. Thus, the output of the 
Monte Carlo analysis is the combined influence of all uncertainties and not only the influence 
of the model in regards to the specific parameters (PRé, Goedkoop, Oele, Vieira, et al., 
2016). The Monte Carlo analysis was conducted for the Base Scenario and Scenario 6 for 
each house and each method in 10,000 simulations. The output of the Monte Carlo analysis is 
the 95% confidence interval within which we are confident the true value lies (PRé, 
Goedkoop, Oele, Leijting, et al., 2016). The results of the uncertainty analysis are presented 
in Section III.IV together with the actual results of our analysis for each house.

therefore tested for sensitivity for all houses in the relevant scenario. Table 11 shows how 
each parameter is tested, for which houses, in which scenario.

A normalised sensitivity coefficient was calculated by perturbation of a parameter with 10% 
and next applying the following equation (Owsianiak et al., 2018): 
 
16     !! = ∆!"#$"#/!"#$"#
∆!"#$%/!"#$%
= ∆!/!
∆!/! 
 
… where ∆y is the change in impact score after the perturbation, y is the impact score before 
the perturbation, ∆x is the difference between the parameter before and after the perturbation 
and x is the parameter value before the perturbation. Where a normalised sensitivity 
==PAGE:66==
 
 
30 
coefficient cannot be calculated, the sensitivity of a parameter was tested by calculating the 
relative difference between before and after a change of the parameter.  
 
The parameters can be divided into four main groups depending on our confidence in the 
parameters (uncertainty) and their influence on the model (sensitivity): Low uncertainty and 
low sensitivity, high uncertainty and low sensitivity, low uncertainty and high sensitivity, and 
high uncertainty and high sensitivity. This is illustrated in Figure 2. If a parameter is found to 
be in the bottom left corner (low uncertainty and low sensitivity), we did not consider it 
further, as we are both very confident in the modelled value and in any case it has little 
influence on the results of the impact assessment. If a parameter was located in the bottom 
right corner (low uncertainty and high sensitivity), it was still not strictly necessary to 
consider the parameter further, as we are very confident in the modelled value, even though 
the parameter has great influence on the results. We similarly disregarded a parameter if it 
was found to be in the top left corner (high uncertainty and low sensitivity), because even 
though we are uncertain of the modelled value, we know that it in any case will not impact 
the results. However, if a parameter was found to be in the top right corner (high uncertainty 
and high sensitivity), we needed to consider the parameter further when interpreting the 
results obtained from the LCIA, as we have very little confidence in a modelled value that 
has great influence on the results.  
 
II.VII.III Life Cycle Impact Assessment 
As described in the Article, two different impact assessment methods were applied in this 
study. It was decided to limit the scope of the study and focus on a selection of impact 
categories, rather than attempt to cover aspects related to all categories. As this study 
considers 6 houses, 7 scenarios, 6 allocation principles and 2 methods, it was found 
reasonable to aim for limited impact coverage. However, the results for all impact categories 
can be found in Supporting Information V. The considered impact categories were chosen 
based on a preliminary study (see Supporting Information II).  
 
Based on a study by Khasreen et al. (2009) it was found that the choice of impact categories 
will in most cases depend on the goal of the study, data availability along with the 
significance of the impacts (Khasreen, Banfill and Menzies, 2009). The findings of Khasreen 
et al. (2009) indicated that the impact category global warming or climate change is highly 
relevant in most LCA studies, as it covers a global scale and furthermore it is a widely 
==PAGE:67==
 
 
31 
discussed topic in most political arenas. Furthermore, the Danish Building Research Institute 
has, as earlier mentioned, carried out an assessment of the CO2 reductions related to each 
house. Therefore, it was decided to include the category Climate change (kg CO2 eq.) for 
CCnorm and the categories Climate change - Energy imbalance (Wm-2) and Climate change - 
CO2 concentration (ppm) for PB-LCIA. Furthermore, it was decided to include impact 
categories that were found to be specifically relevant to the building industry, e.g. land use 
and water depletion. For CCnorm the impact categories Land use (kg C deficit) and Water 
resource depletion (m3 water eq.) were included and for PB-LCIA Land system change - 
Global (%) and Freshwater use - Global (km3) were included. Finally, even though 
eutrophication is not an environmental issue directly connected to the built environment, it 
does represent an important contributor to anthropogenic pressures on river inlets and coastal 
waters, with serious consequences for ecosystem functioning (Desmit et al., 2018). Therefore 
it was decided to include the impact categories Marine eutrophication (kg N eq) and 
Biogeochemical flows - N (Tg N).   
 
 
==PAGE:68==
 
 
32 
III 
Results  
In this section results obtained using CCnorm and PB-LCIA are presented. As not all results 
are relevant to the purpose of the Article, only results comparing the scenarios and the life 
cycle stages are presented in this Section. The remaining results for each house, method, 
scenario, impact category and allocation principle are presented in Supporting Information V.

and impact categories were found. The results obtained using the two methods ILCD 2011 
and PB-LCIA are presented in Table 12 and 13, respectively.

==PAGE:72==
 
 
36 
From Table 12 and 13 it is found that Scenario 6 (the scenario combining all other scenarios) 
is the best performing scenario for both methods and all houses irrespective of impact 
category. Hence, Scenario 6 is the ultimately best case scenario. In addition, Scenario 1 
(examining the environmental impact of the houses with no expansion) and Scenario 3 
(examining the environmental impact of the houses if the energy consumption is reduced by 
25%) are found to generate a low environmental impact across methods, houses and impact 
categories and thus are among the best performing scenarios. The results obtained using 
ILCD 2011 shows that Scenario 1 is the second best performing scenario in terms of 
environmental impact in 14 out of the 24 impact categories across houses, whereas Scenario 
3 is the second best performing scenario in 9 out of 24 impact categories considering all 
houses (see Table 12). To compare when using the PB-LCIA method, Scenario 1 performs 
second best in 21 out of 30 impact categories and considering all houses, whereas Scenario 3 
performs second best in 8 out of 30 impact categories (see Table 13).  
 
In addition, Table 12 and 13 show that when using the method ILCD 2011, the Base Scenario 
(i.e. current conditions) is the worst performing scenario in 11 out of 24 impact categories 
when considering all houses, whereas when using the PB-LCIA method the Base Scenario 
performs the worst in 16 out of 30 impact categories across houses. Thus, the Base Scenario 
is, as expected, the ultimately worst case scenario. However, considering the impact category 
Land use (ILCD 2011), Scenario 2 is found to have the highest impact potential for all houses 
and thus is the worst performing scenario for this impact category. Investigating this result 
further, it is found that the energy consumption in the Use stage is the main contributor to this 
impact category (see Table 14 and 15). As Scenario 2 considers the energy mix of 2030, the 
high impact potential in the impact category Land use could be to due the fact that, as 
previously described, the energy demand will increase in the near future (see Section 
II.VII.II) and thus more energy will be produced which requires more physical space than 
that of today’s. The results presented in Table 12 and 13 support the conclusion stated in the 
Article, namely that the strategies for sustainable design investigated in Scenario 1 and 3 are 
the most effective strategies to lower the environmental impact potential and developing 
towards a more sustainable building design of single family stand-alone dwellings.

40 
From the results it is found that the Use stage is the largest contributor to the environmental 
impact irrespective of which house is considered. When examining the results obtained using 
the LCIA method ILCD 2011, the Use stage is found to have the largest environmental 
impact potential rendering it to be the main contributor to the environmental impact in 18 out 
of 24 impact categories across all houses (see Table 14). To compare, when using the PB-
LCIA method the Use stage is found to be the main contributor in 24 out of 30 impact 
categories across all houses (see Table 15). The large impact potential from the Use stage was 
expected, as the energy consumption is a continuous input and thus keeps adding up during 
the service life of the houses.  
 
Furthermore, the End of Life stage is found to be a large contributor to the environmental 
impact potential particularly in the impact categories Marine eutrophication (ILCD 2011), 
Climate change (ILCD 2011), Climate change - Energy imbalance (PB-LCIA), Climate 
change - CO2 concentration (PB-LCIA) and Biogeochemical flows - N (PB-LCIA). The large 
environmental impact in the End of Life stage emerges from materials being disposed of for 
municipal incineration contributing to impact categories related to climate change and from 
wastewater treatment highly contributing to the impact categories Marine eutrophication and 
Biogeochemical flows - N for all houses. In fact, treatment of wastewater constitutes for 53-
59% of the End of Life stage when considering ILCD 2011, all houses and 83-87% of the 
End of Life stage when considering PB-LCIA. However, as wastewater is a result of the Use 
stage and is a continuous input, this result was expected. Finally, as presented in Table 14 the 
Product stage is found to be the second largest contributor in 13 out of 24 impact categories 
when considering the method ILCD 2011 and in 19 out of 30 impact categories when 
considering the PB-LCIA method (see Table 15). Again, this result was expected and 
indicates that the Product stage as well as the Use stage is important to consider when seeking 
to reduce the environmental impact of single family stand-alone dwellings.  
 
III.III CC and PB available to the buildings 
To investigate if the Mini CO2 houses can be considered absolute sustainable, the available 
SoSOS to a single family house is estimated for each impact category. The SoSOS is 
estimated using six different allocation principles as described in Section II.IV. The shares 
presented in Table 16 and 17 represents the maximum annual impact potential that each of 
the Mini CO2 houses may occupy in order to stay within its share of carrying capacity and 
Planetary Boundaries of the Earth System. For example; regarding the impact category 
==PAGE:77==
 
 
41 
Climate change, the potential impact of each Mini CO2 house may not exceed the limit of 
101 kg CO2 eq/yr for allocation principle 1 in order to be absolute sustainable.

From Table 18 and 19 it is found that the impact score obtained from this study in general lies 
within the 95% confidence interval obtained through the Monte Carlo analysis. For CCnorm, 
44 out of 48 impact scores is within the 95% confidence interval and for PB-LCIA the count 
is 54 out of 60 impact scores. However, considering the method CCnorm, the impact potential 
of Ref House and UP House, Scenario 6 for the two impact categories Marine eutrophication 
and Land use lies outside the 95% confidence interval and thus the model is uncertain. The 
case is the same when considering the PB-LCIA method. The impact potential of Ref House 
for the Base Scenario and Scenario 6 lies outside the 95% confidence interval for the impact 
categories Land-system change - global and Freshwater use - global as well as the impact 
potential of Q House for the Base Scenario and the impact categories Land-system change - 
global and Freshwater use - global. As stated, most impact scores for the tested scenarios lies 
within the 95% confidence interval and thus the model is not considered uncertain to a 
reasonable degree. However, since 4 out of 48 impact scores (for CCnorm) and 6 out of 60 
impact scores (for PB-LCIA) are outside the 95% confidence interval the model represent 
some level of uncertainty. As the Monte Carlo analysis performed in SimaPro calculates the 
inherent uncertainty of the model and not the uncertainty of the model against one specific 
parameter, it is not possible to identify which parameter is uncertain. Thus, the model have 
not been optimised for uncertainty, but instead the uncertainty have been taken into account 
when interpreting the results.

46 
Sensitivity analysis 
In this section the results of the sensitivity analysis are presented. As previously described, 
the tested parameters are the energy use for construction (+10%), the consumption of water 
and heat during the Use stage (+10%) and the service life (+-10%) (see Table 20 and 21). In 
addition, Scenario 1 to 5 are tested relative to the Base Scenario as well as the relative change 
if using stone wool instead of glass wool as process for mineral wool. The relative change for 
these parameters are presented (see Table 22 and 23).

Table 20 and 21 shows that the normalised sensitivity coefficient is below 0.5 for all houses 
and impact categories for the parameters energy use for construction, consumption of energy 
for heating and service life. The only parameter that the model are sensitive towards is the 
consumption of water during the Use stage. The normalised sensitivity coefficient is above 
0.5 for 11 out of 24 impact categories (Marine eutrophication and Water resource depletion) 
for CCnorm and 11 out of 30 impact categories (Biogeochemical flows - N and Freshwater use

and thus the uncertainty of this parameter should be taken into account when interpreting the 
results.

The sensitivity analysis of Scenario 1 to 5 presented in Table 22 and 23 show that the model 
is sensitive towards all parameters tested in the scenarios as all scenarios show a relative 
change above 2% for at least one impact category. However, the model is found to be 
particularly sensitive towards the changes in Scenario 1 and 3, as the relative change of 2% is 
exceeded in 23 out of 24 impact categories (CCnorm) and 29 out 30 impact categories (PB-
LCIA) for Scenario 1 and in 20 out of 24 impact categories (CCnorm) and 24 out of 30 impact 
categories (PB-LCIA) for Scenario 3. This support the conclusion stated in the Article, 
namely that no expansion (Scenario 1) and a reduced energy consumption (Section 3) are 
among the most effective strategies to reduce the environmental impact. In addition, the 
sensitivity analysis show that the model is sensitive to the choice of process for mineral wool 
i.e. modelling mineral wool as glass wool or stone wool. However, as this parameter 
represents a high level of variability, one process can be just-as-likely as the other.  
 
The combined uncertainty and sensitivity of all tested parameters are summarised in Figure 2. 
As previously stated, the parameters located in the upper right corner should be taken into 
account when interpreting the results, as these are the parameters, which are uncertain and 
sensitive and thus potentially could affect the results.  
 
Figure 2 - Combined uncertainty and sensitivity of each parameter tested quantitatively.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:91==
 
 
55 
IV 
Discussion 
The following paragraphs elaborate on aspects of the Article that was found to require further 
explanation or discussion to be sufficiently covered. A more thorough discussion of when and 
how the methods may be compared is given, key uncertain assumptions is discussed and 
finally the results obtained are validated by comparing characterised results with similar 
studies of the Mini CO2 Houses.  
 
IV.I 
Comparing the methods 
Ryberg et al. (2018) investigated the possibility of comparing the impact categories of PB-
LCIA with those of ILCD 2011 by calculating Spearman rank and Pearson correlation 
coefficients. The former was done with absolute impact scores, and a low correlation 
coefficient indicates discrepancy between the rankings of unit processes, while the latter was 
done with ranked impact scores and a low correlation coefficient indicates discrepancy 
between magnitudes of the impact scores for the unit processes (Ryberg, Owsianiak, 
Richardson, et al., 2018).  
 
It was found that the impact categories Climate change (ILCD 2011) and Climate change - 
CO2 concentration (PB-LCIA) have a Pearson correlation coefficient of 1.00 and Spearman 
rank correlation coefficient of 0.99. The same coefficients of correlation were found for 
Climate change (ILCD 2011) and Climate change - Energy imbalance (PB-LCIA).  
For the impact categories Marine eutrophication (ILCD 2011) and Biogeochemical flows - N 
(PB-LCIA) the Pearson correlation coefficient is 0.97 and Spearman rank correlation 
coefficient is 0.95. Ryberg et al. (2018) argue that the two impact categories can reasonably 
be compared, as most LCIA methods consider nitrogen as the primary contributor to Marine 
eutrophication. The impact categories Land use (ILCD 2011) and Land-system change - 
global (PB-LCIA) have the lowest coefficients of correlation even though they both express 
land use. The Pearson correlation coefficient was found to be 0.27 and the Spearman rank 
correlation coefficient to be 0.85 (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018). 
 
The high coefficients for the Spearman rank correlation analysis indicate that the methods 
generally give a similar estimate of which processes contribute most to the environmental 
burdens (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018). The high correlation coefficients for 
impact categories related to climate change can be explained by a similar approach for 
deriving characterisation models for both methods (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 
==PAGE:92==
 
 
56 
2018). The low coefficients of correlation for the impact categories related to land use is due 
to a difference in the focus of protection. The PB-LCIA impact category Land-system change 
- Global only include transformation of forest area, while the ILCD 2011 impact category 
Land use include other changes as well (Laurent and Owsianiak, 2017). Generally, Ryberg  
(2018) concludes that a comparison can be justified if the focus of protection in existing LCA 
impact categories can be compared to those of the PB. However, it should be stressed that - 
as mentioned in the Article - the impact categories of the two methods cannot be matched 1:1 
as they differ in several areas (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018). 
 
A relevant challenge for both methods is how to deal with spatial differentiation of the 
thresholds. The PBs are in many cases related to Earth System processes, which occur at 
local or regional scale, and does not have any known global thresholds. Of the PB-LCIA 
impact categories considered in this study two require spatially differentiation, namely 
Freshwater use and Land-system change. Without spatial differentiation it would be 
necessary to aggregate averages over regions, and this could mean overlooking an 
exceedance of regional thresholds (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 2018). This has 
been solved in PB-LCIA by classifying e.g. freshwater basins into archetypes based on the 
aridity of the geographical area in which the river basins are located. Even though this 
classification ensures that water abundance in humid areas does not hide the exceedance of 
local thresholds in arid regions, it still leaves the risk that exceedance of local level thresholds 
are overlooked due to water abundance in other areas (Ryberg, Owsianiak, Richardson, et al., 
2018). Furthermore, although necessary, the modification of the impact categories weakens 
the link to the PB-framework and complicates relating the impact scores to the SOS.  
 
IV.II Enabling the comparison between the houses 
The main cause of assumptions in this study was the comparative aspect given by the defined 
scope. A main goal was to compare the performance of the five Mini CO2 Houses and to 
allow for this comparison it was necessary to ensure equality between the “service” supplied 
by the houses. This meant that if one house enabled a “service”, e.g. low maintenance, it was 
crucial to assess if and how this could be accounted for in the systems for the other houses. In 
most cases, this was solved by performing system expansion. It was attempted to give each 
house one key benefit, corresponding to their design strategy. UP House was modelled with 
fewer virgin materials, IMF House and TMF House was modelled with a longer service life, 
AD House was modelled with less square meters over the entire service (as it was assumed 
==PAGE:93==
 
 
57 
that the expansion was removed once it could be spared), and Q House was modelled with 
lower energy and water consumption. However, all benefits was assigned with a critical mind 
so as to not overestimate the potential of the design strategies. Both IMF House and TMF 
House has been designed to be “architecturally timeless”, UP House has been designed with 
recycled furniture, and in AD House the interior walls have a flexible location in the house. 
While all of these features are important, they were not found to be verifiable at this point and 
therefore not fair to include as benefits.  
 
Where a benefit was added to a parameter for a house it was attempted to make the remaining 
houses more or less fair within the same parameter. This meant that all houses except UP 
House are modelled with mainly virgin materials, all other houses except IMF and TMF have 
a service life of 120 years, to all houses except AD House a permanent expansion has been 
added (the expansion added AD House is removable), and all houses except Q House has a 
energy and water consumption reflecting a standard Danish single family stand-alone 
dwelling. Limiting the amount of variables between the comparisons was adopted to 
highlight the environmental impact potential of each design strategy as much as possible. 
 
Although the modelled systems for the houses all fulfil the defined functional unit, it is 
relevant to consider if they can actually be fairly compared. Dwellings are complex systems 
to model, and while the primary function is clear - provide a liveable space - a wide range of 
secondary functions exist and challenge the comparison. Even though a primitive cabin 
would fulfil the functional unit of housing one family, most people choose to live in 
comfortable modern houses even though they are more expensive and require more 
maintenance. Architectural design (appearance, size, floor layout) will affect whether or not a 
house is considered a suitable living option for many people and as this is inherently a matter 
of personal taste, how can two non-identical houses really be compared? In this study it was 
assumed that all houses were acceptable alternatives to one another and that the matter of 
personal taste in building design could be overlooked.  
 
IV.III Service life of the building and building materials  
As described in the Article, one of the main uncertainties of the modelled systems is the 
assumed service life of the building components. In the following the considerations behind 
the choice of modelling approach will be elaborated. The service life of building components 
can be considered in four ways: technical, functional, financial and aesthetic. Technical 
==PAGE:94==
 
 
58 
service life refers to the time from when the building component is installed in the building 
while still having the physical and technical ability to fulfil its intended function. Technical 
service life is determined by the external forces on the component and its ability to withstand 
such forces (Aagaard et al., 2013). Functional service life is defined as the time from when 
the building component is installed in the building until the original function of the 
component is no longer required (Aagaard et al., 2013). Financial service life is the time 
from when the building component is installed in the building until maintenance and 
replacements are no longer reasonable from a life cycle cost perspective (Aagaard et al., 
2013). Aesthetic service life describes the time from when the building component is installed 
in the building and while the aesthetic standard is maintained (Aagaard et al., 2013). 
 
The service lives of all building components were in this study modelled as technical. 
Furthermore, it was decided to allow the service life of the entire building to dictate the 
modelled service life for all building components not exposed to either wear and tear (indoor 
surfaces) or the elements (outdoor surfaces) as it was assumed that they would be either too 
complicated or expensive to replace. These assumptions allowed the choice of materials to 
influence the impact from maintenance of the building, thereby giving the maintenance-free 
houses their intended advantage. However, it can be argued that the three other types of 
service life definitions should have influenced the modelled service life as well. Financial 
service life was not considered, as it was found to be outside the scope of this study and 
functional- as well as aesthetic service life was found unsuitable for quantification, as it is 
inherently defined by socio-technical and psychological factors (Aagaard et al., 2013). 
 
IV.IV Validating the results  
Table 24 compares results from similar studies of the Mini CO2 Houses from the Danish 
Building Research Institute (Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir, 2013) and Brejnrod et al. (2017) 
with the results obtained in this study for the impact category Climate change (or global 
warming potential in the Ökobau-framework).

It is clear that the results obtained in this study overall corresponds well with results obtained 
by others using different impact assessment methods. The greatest difference is AD House 
for which this study found an annual climate change impact of 2112.7 kg CO2-eq compared 
to the study by the Danish Building Research Institute (Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir, 2013) 
which only estimated 1231.2 kg CO2-eq annually. However, this difference can be explained 
by the fact that the latter does not include an expansion of the house, which has been 
modelled as occurring three times during the lifetime of the building in this study. 
Furthermore, the Reference House has a somewhat lower impact in the study by the Danish 
Building Research Institute (Rasmussen and Birgisdóttir, 2013) than in this study, and this 
can be explained by the fact that the data used to model in the inventory has been updated in 
the time period between the two studies.
