==PAGE:101==
Assessment of absolute sustainability in the built environment 
 
 
Supporting Information II 
 
 
Pernille Krogh Ohms1 & Camilla Marlene Ernst Andersen1 
1Technical University of Denmark, Department of Civil Engineering, Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark 
 
25th of January 2019 
 
 
 
==PAGE:102==
Reading guide 
A project course prior to this thesis was conducted in August 2018. Through this project, we sought 
to investigate the subject of absolute sustainability and to identify methodologies to quantify 
absolute sustainability. The study was conducted as a literature review where relevant literature 
regarding absolute sustainability were examined including the inevitable topics of carrying capacity 
of the Earth System and the Planetary Boundaries framework. Furthermore, 12 methodologies to 
assess the level of absolute sustainability were examined and of those, two methodologies were 
chosen to be applied in this Master thesis. This literature review resulted in a report briefly 
describing absolute sustainability and related methodologies as well as discussing pros and cons of 
each method when to be applied in a building context. This study is presented in the following and 
the methods identified as the most relevant were applied in the thesis.  
 
 
==PAGE:103==
 
 
 
 
 
Review and analysis  
of methods for absolute sustainability assessments 
 
By Camilla Andersen, s133757 and Pernille Ohms s133740 
24th of August 2018 
 
 
==PAGE:104==
1 Introduction 
This report is a preliminary study to create the basis for a master's thesis which will be conducted in 
the fall of 2018 in collaboration with the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The thesis will 
treat the subject of absolute environmental sustainability in the building industry using the 
methodology of life cycle assessment (LCA). To support this, this study seeks to describe and 
investigate the basic principles in regards to absolute environmental sustainability including 
planetary boundaries and carrying capacity and to assess existing methods to quantify absolute 
environmental sustainability. These methods will briefly be described and some methods will be 
selected for further investigation and coupled with LCAs in the master’s thesis. Since one of the 
main goals in the master’s thesis will be to compare two methods, pros and cons of the methods 
investigated in this study will be discussed. Finally, the two methods with the most relevance to 
LCAs of buildings will be selected and applied in the master’s thesis.  
  
In addition, when conducting a life cycle assessment, one of the main tasks is to critically examine 
which impact categories are most relevant to the specific case. In this study, the focus is on the 
building industry, which is why one of the goals of this report is to evaluate which impact 
categories are the most relevant to include when performing LCAs on buildings.   
2 Absolute sustainability  
Along with an increasing interest in environmental sustainability of products and services the recent 
years have seen a growing use of life cycle assessment (LCA) and life cycle thinking in many 
industries as well as the public sector (Bjørn and Hauschild, 2015). However, LCA is inherently 
most suitable in comparative assessments. The methodology does not indicate how a product 
performs relative to a specific target, it can only reveal whether product “A” has a higher or lower 
environmental impact than product “B” (Brejnrod et al., 2017). This inspires the need for an 
“absolute” evaluation criteria which identifies the ability of the environment to cope with human 
activities. Various environmental indicators have been defined for global problems, but these are 
typically only used to compare relative performance and are rarely used to define good or bad 
performance above or below an absolute boundary (Nykvist et al., 2013). In the following 
paragraphs two different indicators of absolute sustainability will be presented and discussed.  
 
==PAGE:105==
2.1 Defining the planetary boundaries 
Over the past 10,000 years the Earth has remained within a stable domain known as the Holocene 
with only natural environment fluctuations (Rockström et al., 2009). Our planet’s resilience has 
kept it within a state where key Earth system processes can take place and until recently humans 
have not affected the functioning of the Earth system at a global scale (Rockström et al., 2009). The 
industrial revolution of the 18th and 19th century changed this. Human activities since then have 
increasingly influenced the Earth’s climate and ecosystems and this has caused the Earth to enter a 
new domain, the Anthropocene (Rockström et al., 2009). The fact that this change in domain is so 
clearly linked to human activities inspired the question posed by Rockström et al. in their 2009 
article: “What are the non-negotiable planetary preconditions that humanity needs to respect in 
order to avoid the risk of deleterious or even catastrophic environmental change at continental to 
global scale?”. This led to the concept of planetary boundaries which was first presented in 2009. 
Planetary boundaries mark a safe operating space for humanity with respect to the Earth’s system 
and in association with the planets biophysical subsystems and processes (Brejnrod et al., 2017). 
The planetary boundaries defined by Rockström et al. (2009) were - and still is - associated with a 
high level of uncertainty. As the planetary boundaries are interlinked, crossing one limit may 
drastically reduce the limit of other planetary boundaries, and this makes it almost impossible to 
predict cases of burden shifting between environmental indicators (Dao et al., 2015).  
  
The concept of planetary boundaries is one of the most recent attempts to quantify the carrying 
capacity of our planet at a global scale. Carrying capacity can be defined as the maximum sustained 
environmental intervention a natural system can withstand without experiencing negative changes 
in structure or functioning that are difficult or impossible to revert (Bjørn and Hauschild, 2015). 
Both carrying capacity and planetary boundaries can be defined as “limits” of our Earth system, not 
to be confused with policy targets such as Millennium Development Goals (MDGs) and Sustainable 
Development Goals (SDGs) or with footprints such as carbon footprint and water footprint (Dao et 
al., 2015). The limit indicating carrying capacity of the Earth is not the same as the limit indicating 
planetary boundaries. The carrying capacity-limit is specific for each local system and it is therefore 
not possible to know the exact location of a global carrying capacity-limit as this will be affected by 
many local systems (Ryberg, 2018). This is expressed by a zone of uncertainty surrounding the 
unknown carrying capacity-limit. The planetary boundary, which relates to a global system, is 
==PAGE:106==
placed at the lower limit of the zone of uncertainty. This ensures that planetary boundaries 
effectively define the safe operating space for humanity (Ryberg, 2018).  
2.2 Sharing the safe operating space  
One limitation in the definition of the planetary boundaries is the lack of spatial differentiation. For 
example, for the land use boundary it is only stated that, globally, no more than 15% of ice-free 
land must be converted to cropland, but it is not specified which land would be more or less harmful 
to convert (Nykvist et al., 2013). This universal approach underlines the relevance of downscaling 
the planetary boundaries to a regional scale, or in other words perform an allocation of the “safe 
operating space”. Furthermore, the relevance of regional-scale boundaries is reinforced by an 
argument towards guaranteeing equity among all humans. However, allocating the world’s carrying 
capacity to countries or regions cannot be done in a truly objective way. Any allocation method can 
be perceived as subjective and thereby more or less fair depending on the eyes of the beholder 
(Brejnrod et al., 2017). Performing an allocation based on an egalitarian approach where the 
carrying capacity of the earth is equally distributed among each world citizen is a popular approach 
to the dilemma.  
  
Incorporating absolute sustainability in terms of planetary boundaries and carrying capacity into a 
LCA can be done by including one of the following three components: one is to develop 
characterization factors for impact assessment based on the carrying capacity of the Earth, another 
is to carry out a normalization by applying factors based on carrying capacity and finally, the share 
of the planetary boundaries occupied by the corresponding footprint can be used as a weighting 
factor (Brejnrod et al., 2017). Both approaches would affect the impact assessment step of the 
traditional LCA methodology where characterization and normalization should take place according 
to ISO 14040. In the following paragraphs 12 different methods that are all related to the 
quantification of absolute sustainability is investigated in terms of their relevance towards coupling 
LCA with absolute sustainability. Some of the methods are designed to be included in a step of a 
typical LCA, whereas some methods explain a whole new approach to life cycle assessment.  Some 
of the methods are designed to be included in a step of a typical LCA, whereas some methods 
explain a whole new approach to life cycle assessment.   
 
==PAGE:107==
3 Methods  
Method 1: Techno-Ecological Synergy Life Cycle Assessment (TES-LCA) 
The methodology Techno-Ecological Synergy Life Cycle Assessment (TES-LCA) is an approach 
presented by Liu and Bakshi (2018) in which each step of a conventional LCA is modified. As 
mentioned, a conventional LCA is relative and thus, to measure absolute environmental 
sustainability, a comparison between supply and demand of ecosystem services (ES) is needed (Liu 
and Bakshi, 2018). The TES framework includes the ES demanded by technological systems and 
the supply from the corresponding ecosystems. From this, the ecological exceedance of the 
corresponding carrying capacity of technological systems can be estimated. By combining TES 
with LCA, it is possible to measure the extent of absolute sustainability of a product, system or 
service and thereby help in decision making from a local to a global scale (Liu and Bakshi, 2018). 
The framework allows for two kinds of allocation principles, namely proportional allocation and 
avoided allocation, which differs in such a way that proportional allocation can provide local 
sustainability metrics, whereas avoided allocation assumes all activities to have identical 
sustainability metrics (Liu and Bakshi, 2018). 
  
Method 2: Emissions intensity based on emission allowances 
In Girod et al. (2013), the goal is to assess the required change in emission intensity to achieve 
global emission reduction goals. First, the future global consumption patterns are estimated and 
then the IPAT equation is used to estimate the related effects on GHG (Greenhouse Gas) emissions. 
IPAT is expressed as Impact = Population x Affluence x Technology (IPAT), where the Impact is 
in GHG emissions, Affluence in consumption per capita, Technology in emissions per unit of 
consumption for different consumption categories. The next step is to allocate the emissions to the 
different consumption categories in order to finally estimate the GHG intensity based on the 
emission allowances (Girod, Van Vuuren and Hertwich, 2013). By doing so, the emission intensity 
changes required to reach global emission goals can be found and then used in a decision-making 
processes and climate mitigation. The framework presented by Girod et al. focuses on global 
consumption levels and the five different consumption categories food, shelter, travel, goods and 
services (Girod, Van Vuuren and Hertwich, 2013).  
 
 
  
==PAGE:108==
Method 3: Carrying capacity-based normalization factors 
Bjørn et al. (2015) presents a framework that links carrying capacity of an ecosystem with impacts 
found in a LCA through development of carrying capacity-based normalization references. These 
carrying capacity-based normalization references is based on scientific thresholds identified in 
existing literature and is described in annual personal share of carrying capacity. Using these 
normalization references enables an assessment of absolute environmental sustainability on a local, 
regional or global scale. In Bjørn et al. (2015) average carrying capacity-based normalization 
references on a global scale (the world) and on a regional scale (Europe) is developed and the 
normalization references is developed for ten midpoint categories typically included in LCA and 
linked to the area of protection natural environment. These midpoint categories are climate change, 
ozone depletion, photochemical ozone formation, terrestrial acidification, terrestrial eutrophication, 
freshwater eutrophication, marine eutrophication, ecotoxicity, land use, and water depletion.   
  
Method 4: Planetary boundaries-based weighting factors 
In Tuomisto et al. (2012) a framework for the weighting step in a LCA, which is usually considered 
unreliable, uncertain and subjective, is presented. To cope with this subjectiveness and uncertainty, 
the framework is based on the nine planetary boundaries described by Rockström et al. (2009), 
namely climate change, rate of biodiversity loss, nitrogen cycle, phosphorus cycle, stratospheric 
ozone depletion, global freshwater use, and change in land use (Tuomisto et al., 2012). The 
planetary boundary for each category is used to develop the weighting factors by calculating the 
ratio between the current status of environmental impact and the estimated boundary (Tuomisto et 
al., 2012). By doing so, it is assumed that all impact categories are of equal importance. This is an 
important assumption, however it is consistent with the planetary boundary framework that gives 
the safe boundaries which it is necessary to stay within (Tuomisto et al., 2012). The weighting 
factors, the result of the impact before weighting and the normalization value, are then used to 
calculate the total weighted score for all impact categories (Tuomisto et al., 2012). 
  
Method 5: Biophysical Accounting  
In the study by Fanning and O’Neill (2016), indicators that measure the performance of high-
consuming nations are developed. A good or bad performance is in relation to the planetary 
boundaries framework and the defined safe boundaries. As Fanning and O’Neill (2016) explains, 
nations that performs well meets the conditions of a steady-state economy, which means having 
==PAGE:109==
non-growing physical stocks and flows. Fanning et al. develops 10 different indicators to describe 
the performance, which includes three indicators of aggregate resource consumption, three 
measures of physical stock and four measures of sustainable scale (Fanning and O’Neill, 2016). The 
four measures of sustainable scale are cumulative carbon footprint relative to carbon budget, 
nutrient use relative to biogeochemical boundaries, blue water consumption relative to monthly 
basin-level availability and land footprint relative to biocapacity (Fanning and O’Neill, 2016). In 
Fanning and O’Neill (2016), the framework is applied to two national scales and two sub-national 
scales and can be helpful in prioritizing environmental decision-making.   
  
Method 6: Footprint-boundary ESA (F-B ESA) 
Fang et al. (2015) presents an update of the footprint-boundary ESA (F-B ESA) framework. The 
aim with the updated framework is to show that the framework is applicable on a national level to 
allocate responsibility for environmental sustainability (Fang et al., 2015). The updated framework 
is based on the concept of planetary boundaries for three indicators, namely carbon emissions, 
water use and land use (Fang et al., 2015). These boundaries are allocated to 28 selected countries 
and the environmental footprint of these countries are compared (Fang et al., 2015). To measure the 
performance of the selected countries in regards to the planetary boundaries, two measures are 
developed. One, Environmental Sustainability Distance (ESD), refers to the difference between the 
footprint of a nation and the relevant safe boundary. If the difference, ESD, is above zero the 
country is considered unsustainable, since the footprint exceeds the safe boundary and vice versa 
(Fang et al., 2015). Another measure, ESR, refers to the ratio of the footprint of a country to a 
relevant safe boundary. As for the measure ESD, if the ratio, ESR, is above zero the country is 
considered unsustainable, since the footprint exceeds the safe boundary and vice versa (Fang et al., 
2015). 
  
Method 7: Safe and just space for humanity (SJS) 
The planetary boundaries framework was extended in 2012 by Raworth to feature indicators of 
social well-being. This framework is called “safe and just space for humanity” (SJS) and includes 
11 social dimensions in addition to the nine planetary boundaries. Since the main aim with this 
method is to help identify priority areas for action and to influence public policy on a national scale 
(Cole, Bailey and New, 2014), the goal presented in Cole, Bailey and New (2014) is to downscale 
the SJS framework to a national level and use it to measure the social and environmental 
==PAGE:110==
sustainability of South Africa. The safe boundaries for the 20 social and environmental indicators 
are based on data from national and international databases and reports, academic papers and expert 
judgement and the ratio between the footprint and the boundary is presented in two radar charts 
(Cole, Bailey and New, 2014). 
  
Method 8: Carrying capacity-based characterization factors  
Bjørn et al. (2016) presents a way of using carrying capacity as environmental sustainability 
reference in life cycle impact assessment models. This is a framework to integrate absolute 
environmental sustainability in LCAs and to express occupation of carrying capacity per kg 
emission for each impact category (Bjørn et al., 2016). To calculate the characterization factors, 
Bjørn et al. (2016) coupled carrying capacities with deposition factors from a global deposition 
mode. This is done by dividing conventional characterization factors such as the fate factor (FF), 
the effect factor (EF) and the exposure factor (XF) by carrying capacity for the LCA impact 
categories. By doing so new characterization factors can be used further in the LCA (Bjørn et al., 
2016). The framework is tested for the impact category terrestrial acidification and the study 
therefore covers the global scale. However, whether the framework applies to a local, regional or 
global scale depends on the spatial dimension of the LCA impact category.  
  
Method 9: Household-level Sustainability Transition (HST) 
This framework, presented by Laakso and Lettenmeier (2016), describes a way to transition towards 
sustainability on a household-level. The framework is based on the method Material Input Per unit 
of Service (MIPS), which can be used to indicate the use of material resources on a household level 
(Laakso and Lettenmeier, 2016). The Household-level Sustainability Transition (HST) expands the 
MIPS methodology by not only including the resource use but also including vision-development, 
conducting experiments, and learning and upscaling (Laakso and Lettenmeier, 2016). In this 
framework the resource use of households is measured on a micro level and tested towards a one-
planet resource use (Laakso and Lettenmeier, 2016). In (Laakso and Lettenmeier, 2016), the 
framework is tested on five households in Finland and different strategies for each household is 
developed by the users along with providers of services, products and infrastructures. It is clear that 
the spatial scale of this framework is on a local level, however if applied to a significant number of 
households it will reduce the global resource use (Laakso and Lettenmeier, 2016). 
  
==PAGE:111==
Method 10: Impact reduction targets in LCA context 
Sandin, Peters and Svanström (2015) presents a methodology to set impact-reduction targets for 
products using the global goals defined in the planetary boundaries framework. This methodology 
is meant to be integrated in a typical life cycle assessment (LCA) of products and thereby provide 
the opportunity to evaluate the potential for intervention to lower the environmental impact of the 
examined product (Sandin, Peters and Svanström, 2015). The framework consists of four steps, 
which is described in Sandin, Peters and Svanström (2015) thoroughly. First step is to identify the 
planetary boundaries that is relevant to the impact categories examined for the specific product. 
Next, to interpret global impact-reduction targets implied by planetary boundaries. Third, is to 
translate the planetary boundaries into reduction targets for the global market segment that the 
product belongs. Finally, the global market reduction targets are translated into production-specific 
reduction targets (Sandin, Peters and Svanström, 2015). This framework was tested on the Swedish 
clothing industry and in doing so (Sandin, Peters and Svanström, 2015) found that the outcome is 
strongly dependent on the allocation method used to allocate the allowed impact between regions. 
The outcome also greatly depends on the geographical scope, however (Sandin, Peters and 
Svanström, 2015) suggests that the framework can be applied to a global and regional scale.  
  
Method 11: Planetary Boundaries Life Cycle Impact Assessment (PB-LCIA) 
Ryberg et al. (2018) has developed a methodology, PB-LCIA, which allows for expressing 
environmental impacts indicators in metrics that correspond to the control variables in the planetary 
boundaries framework. This methodology enables a quantification of the environmental 
performance of products and technologies in relation to planetary boundaries (Ryberg et al., 2018). 
Ryberg et al. (2018) have developed characterization factors for direct use in the LCIA phase of a 
life cycle assessment (LCA) for 85 elementary flows, all recognized as dominant contributors to 
transgressing specific planetary boundaries. The motivation for developing such as methodology is 
the fact that environmental impacts in a conventional LCIA phase are integrated over time and do 
not describe a specific point in time. This makes LCIA results suitable for comparative purposes, 
but not for expressing changes in environmental states or flow rates (Ryberg et al., 2018). To 
accommodate for this difference, it is necessary to alter the way the functional unit (FU) is 
expressed. The PB-LCIA method allows for a quantification of the share of the “safe operating 
space” that human activities occupy.  
  
==PAGE:112==
Method 12: Planetary Boundaries Allowance (PBA’06) 
This framework, presented in Doka (2016), is based on planetary boundaries concept and is an 
update of the Life Cycle Impact Assessment (LCIA) method. It is called Planetary Boundaries 
Allowance (PBA) since it looks into the environmental impact of a personal consumption pattern 
and checks if this stays within the safe boundary (Doka, 2016). To do so, the global planetary 
boundaries need to be converted into a personal scale of per-capita-allowances (Doka, 2016). From 
this, Doka (2016) develops LCIA characterization factors for each planetary boundary, which 
describes whether or not the safe boundary is exceeded. The unit of these characterization factors is 
PBA per unit of environmental exchange and if this value is above 1, the per-capita annual 
allowance for each boundary is exceeded and the lifestyle is considered unsustainable (Doka, 2016). 
The characterization factors are based on the planetary boundaries and therefore only provide 
generic global characterization factors. This means that it cannot be used on a regional or local level 
(Doka, 2016).  
4 Discussion  
The previous paragraph investigates 12 different methods that were initially found to be linked to 
either planetary boundaries, absolute sustainability, carrying capacity, or life cycle assessments. The 
overall goal of this study is to implement absolute sustainability in life cycle assessment on 
buildings. We find that some of the methods are more relevant than others towards achieving this 
goal. Some methods, such as Method 6, 7 and 10, have a scope either too wide (Method 6 considers 
national scale) or too narrow (Method 7 considers humans, Method 10 considers products) which 
did not match our goal of evaluating at single-building level. Other methods, such as Method 1 and 
2, focus on impacts that did not match our goal of including the importance of the ecosphere 
(Method 1 focuses on the technosphere) or our goal of expanding the scope to several impact 
categories (Method 2 only focuses on GHG emissions). Finally, some methods, such as Method 4, 
were found to be too simple or more or less irrelevant to our goal as they only consider e.g. the 
weighting step of a LCA.  
  
Among the 12 methods described in the previous paragraph we found two methods highly relevant 
to our goal and two methods possibly relevant. The two methods we found to be the most relevant 
were Method 3 and Method 11. Method 3 calculates normalization factors based on carrying 
capacity, and thereby links the carrying capacity of an ecosystem with the impacts found in a LCA. 
==PAGE:113==
Method 11 calculates characterization factors (CFs) that enables a quantification of the 
environmental performance of products and technologies in relation to planetary boundaries. We 
find that it would be interesting to implement both methods in a LCA process to investigate the 
difference between incorporating absolute in sustainability in the form of CFs versus normalization 
factors. Additionally, we find that Method 8 and Method 9 could be interesting to include in our 
future work. Method 8 has the drawback that carrying capacity is expressed as acid deposition, 
which makes it impractical in a comparison with Method 11 as the planetary boundaries does not 
consider this impact category. The scope of Method 9 makes it somewhat relevant to the goal of our 
study. It focuses on the household-scale, but as it focuses more on the behavior of the user rather 
than the technical composition of a building it is not directly applicable in our investigation. 
However, we would like to keep the method in mind as one of the houses we will be investigating, 
is focused on user behavior more than the actual construction of the building.  
4.1 Choosing impact categories  
One of the goals of this initial investigation was to evaluate which impact categories are the most 
relevant to include when performing LCAs on buildings and the built environment. The choice of 
impact categories will in most cases depend on the goal of the study, data availability along with the 
significance of the impacts (Khasreen, Banfill and Menzies, 2009). Khasreen, Banfill and Menzies 
(2009) has carried out a literature review in which they investigate which impact categories are used 
in 25 LCA studies within the building sector in Europe and the USA over the past 15 years. They 
found that the most commonly used impacts were global warming, acidification, eutrophication, 
and ozone depletion. The studies cover several different types of LCAs related to the building 
sector, not all of them relevant to the context of our study. Of the 25 studies described by Khasreen, 
Banfill and Menzies (2009), three were found to be comparable in nature to our study. All three 
considers the impact category energy consumption and two out of the three consider the impacts 
categories global warming potential, photochemical ozone creation, water consumption, depletion 
of biotic resource, acidification, human toxicity, waste creation, eco-toxicity, and eutrophication. 
One of the three cases considers all the studied impact categories (Khasreen, Banfill and Menzies, 
2009).  
  
It is clear that choosing impact categories when performing LCAs on buildings is not a simple task. 
Even though Khasreen, Banfill and Menzies (2009) found that some impacts are considered more 
==PAGE:114==
often than others it is not certain that these categories necessarily are the most relevant nor correct 
to use. The fact that four impact categories are used more often that others might be caused by 
“habits” or “routines” within the LCA community, i.e. that these impact categories are the most 
“popular” to use. It is in no way certain that the four most common impacts will benefit us in 
achieving the goal of our study. Furthermore, the study by Khasreen, Banfill and Menzies (2009) 
does not include impacts such as land use, which has only recently been recognized as an impact 
category. Finally, the geographical scope of our study should be reflected in the choice of impact 
categories, as some impacts affect the environment on a global scale, some on a regional scale, and 
some on a local scale.  
  
At this early point in our study a suggestion towards choosing impact categories is still very 
uncertain and may change several times before the final choice is made. Generally, it seems that the 
impact category global warming or climate change is highly relevant in most LCA studies, it covers 
a global scale and furthermore it is a widely discussed topic in most political arenas. Additionally, 
we expect that impacts related to primary energy demand or energy consumption will be important 
when comparing housing types, especially when a temporal scope is included considering the 
possible change in energy mix over the coming years. Finally, we would suggest including 
categories such as land use, water consumption, and waste creation as these, to the best of our 
knowledge, could be related to the environmental impact of the building industry.     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:115==
5 Conclusions 
The goal of this study was to describe and investigate the basic principles in regards to absolute 
environmental sustainability including planetary boundaries and carrying capacity. Furthermore, we 
wished to assess existing methods to quantify absolute environmental sustainability. Based on the 
investigations carried out in the previous paragraphs we can conclude the following:  
  
·       There is a need for an absolute measure of sustainability that can be incorporated into life 
cycle assessments (LCA). 
·       Human activities has pushed the Earth out of its so far stable state, the Holocene, which 
raises the question of how far we can push our planet before catastrophic and possibly 
irreversible changes happen.  
·       Carrying capacity and planetary boundaries are two ways of defining the limits of our 
planet, but they do however not represent the same limit.  
·       It is highly relevant to consider a downscaling of the planetary boundaries, but this 
requires an allocation which can be difficult to perform while remaining objective.  
·       Absolute sustainability can be incorporated into LCA by the addition of one of three 
components: characterization factors, normalization factors or weighting factors.  
·       Method 3, carrying capacity-based normalization factors, and method 11, Planetary 
Boundaries Life Cycle Impact Assessment, were found to be the most relevant, since these 
methods represent two different steps in LCA which is comparable and used to assess 
absolute sustainability. 
·       It was found that method 8, carrying capacity-based characterization factors, and method 
9, Household-level Sustainability Transition, also could be relevant to include in LCAs of 
buildings.  
·       The most commonly used impacts is global warming, acidification, eutrophication, and 
ozone depletion. 
·       From studying LCAs of building, it was found that the impact categories global warming, 
energy consumption, land use, water consumption, and waste creation seems like the most 
relevant to include in a LCA of single-family houses.

==PAGE:156==
 
i 
Closing statement from the authors 
This Master’s thesis sought to investigate the potential of two novel approaches incorporating 
absolute sustainability into the LCA-methodology by applying both to a case of Danish single 
family stand-alone dwellings i.e. the Mini CO2 Houses. One of the main goals of this report is to 
investigate the environmental potential of the design strategies demonstrated in the Mini CO2 
Houses, as well as evaluating the importance and potential of absolute environmental sustainability 
assessments as we strive to reduce our impact on the environment. While the chosen case was ideal 
for testing the two methods, it is important to note that the Mini CO2 Houses was designed to 
reduce CO2-emissions and does not cover all existing approaches to sustainable building design. 
The findings of this study emphasize the extent of the environmental challenges we are facing, and 
the results obtained with the two methods highlight the distance to the target. 
  
A key finding of this report was that every action counts. This has remained a constant topic 
throughout the process of writing this thesis. The Quota House serves as an example of how 
consumers gain a stronger sense of responsibility when considering the state of the environment. It 
is becoming increasingly clear that consumers have a part to play and hold the power to demand 
alternatives to traditional un-sustainable products and services. According to specialist in 
environmental psychology, Stine Emilie Fløe Dalby, climate change is the result of the greatest case 
of procrastination ever seen (Dalby, 2019). In a feature article in the Danish newspaper 
‘Information’ she accuses politicians and businesses of procrastinating when important actions need 
to be taken towards sustainable development. She claims that as long as politicians keep 
procrastinating, the state of the environment will remain a matter of individual consumer choices, 
instead of a shared motivation and commitment to actual actions and improvements (Dalby, 2019).  
  
As was the conclusion of this study, it is clear that both politicians, businesses and consumers all 
have a responsibility and every actor need to contribute if we are to change our current course 
towards a much warmer planet. The IPCC is clear on the matter; the window for limiting global 
warming to 1.5˚C is narrow – it will require that net global emissions of carbon dioxide is decreased 
with about 45 percent by 2030 – but it is achievable. To quote the IPCC Chair Hoesung Lee’s 
opening statement at COP24: “Every bit of warming matters, every year matters, every choice 
matters” (IPCC Secratariat, 2018).
